# Docker Essential Training

> Docker is one of the most popular container storage solutions in the world. In this comprehensive course, software engineer Carlos Nunez covers everything you need to know to use Docker to build and test your applications. Join Carlos to learn how to build a Dockerfile, along with other important topics like operating containers, Docker networking, Docker storage, the Docker Registry, and some adv

You've gotten really good at starting containers from images in the Docker Hub and building Docker images for your own apps. However, you need to know more. How does this all actually work? What else can be done with Docker? And, what is Docker in Docker? My name is Carlos Nunez. I've helped hundreds of engineers get their production apps into containers across many different industries. And I'm excited to teach you the skills you need to do the same. If you're ready to get your essential containerization journey started, join me in Docker Essential training.

### 1. Docker Explained

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Docker recap](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-recap?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-recap?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's start off with a brief overview of what Docker is and how it works. Getting code working properly across multiple machines can be challenging for multiple reasons. Sometimes the operating system and application was written and tested in differs from the OS it will eventually land up in, or maybe your app might make assumptions on [[Hardware]] files or other properties that might not be true on other machines. Or maybe your app depends on hardware that's on your machine, but not others. Several tools were born to solve this hairy problem. [[Configuration Management]] tools like Chef, [[Ansible]], and Puppet addressed it by using configuration as code to allow admins and developers to bring groups of machines to a desired state. Unfortunately, this came with its own set of challenges. You often needed to install stuff on the machines being managed by these tools before the tools could configure them. Even though these tools were based on Ruby or [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]], we'll forget the one that was based on C++. You'll need to learn markup languages and frameworks, on top of whatever tech stack you will already using. HashiCorp solved this problem with Vagrant a tool that gives developers the ability to bring up and configure pre-made [[Virtual Machines]] with HCL, HashiCorp Configuration Language. That's actually what HCL stands for. Cool, right? However, virtual machines took up lots of resources and often needed additional configuration after they was started. In fact, many Vagrant users use configuration management tools to configure VMs created by Vagrant. All of this made writing code more cumbersome and time consuming.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-recap?u=76281980&t=93)** Dockers took a different approach. Docker uses images and containers to package and run applications anywhere. Containers combine control groups and name spaces. Two [[Linux]] kernel features to create virtual operating system Instances that from the outside look like any other application on your machine. Images are snapshots of file systems that are smashed together and configured to look like a single file system to the containers that use them. Containers and images are not new. From truths, to zones, to Linux containers, these ideas have been around for a very long time. Docker makes these concepts significantly easier to use and understand in three key ways. First, containers and images are configured with the lightweight configuration file format called the Docker file. Second, images can be shared with others and retrieved by other machines to container registries. And third, its command line client uses APIs and systems black magic to make creating and operating containers a breeze. Putting this together, instead of configuring machines deploying your apps into them, and hoping for the best you use Docker files to build and configure your apps into docker images that containers will be created from. Since those images have everything your app needs to successfully run, your app will behave the same regardless of the machine that it's on. Pretty nifty stuff. Now before we move on let's talk about what docker containers are not. Many people think that containers are smaller virtual machines.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-recap?u=76281980&t=185)** They are not. Let's take a look at some key differences. Virtual machines use software called hypervisors to bridge hardware emulated in software with real hardware underneath. For all intents and purposes, they are real computers. Now, because they are real computers, they can run multiple apps at once and they can't see or interact with the hosts that they run on. We've mentioned earlier that containers use control groups and namespaces. Namespaces specify what containers can access on a Linux system and control group specify how much of a particular resource containers can consume. Containers work with container run times to configure these properties. Containers do not emulate hardware. They use the same hardware as any other app on your machine. As a result, containers can potentially interact with your hosts, but the good news is that you can control how containers interact with their hosts and we'll learn more about that later in this course. Finally, containers are designed to only run a single app at a time. There are ways around this though and we'll learn about that later too.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Hardware]] (6), [[Virtual Machines]] (4), [[Linux]] (3), [[Configuration Management]] (2), [[Ansible]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (7), make (2), ansible (1), ruby (1), python (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (5), install (1), you'll need (1)
> **Env Vars:** hcl (2)

#### [Container runtimes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/container-runtimes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/container-runtimes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the last video, we learned that Docker containers are composed of control groups and namespaces. Namespaces define what resources containers can access on its host and control groups define how much of those resources containers can consume, but how does that actually happen? As you can see here, there are a few steps involved in creating a "container". In fact, this is how some engineering teams created their containers before Docker became mainstream. Let's go on a journey and explore the life of a container. First, you'll need to mount a directory for the container's file system. This directory would ideally contain all the files and directories your app needs to run. Since this container will think it's running its own instance of [[Linux]], the directory structure within this directory will need to look very similar to a standard Linux file system. Next, you'll need to create a user namespace to run your application in. This is done with the unshare Linux application which unsurprisingly calls the unshare Linux system call or syscall. This namespace creates a sandbox that your application will run in that restricts what the application can do with their hosts. These restrictions can be removed by unsharing system namespaces along with user namespaces. To further isolate your app and prevent it from modifying real files on your file system, you can use the chroot command shown here to set the directory we created earlier as the fake file system for your app.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/container-runtimes?u=76281980&t=94)** If we were creating our own containers from scratch, you would do this when you run the unshare command mentioned above as you can see on the third line on this code block here. Now that we have a namespace for our app and a fake file system for it to play in, our container is finally ready. However, it would be nice if we could prevent our app from, say consuming all of our system's memory or CPU. We can do this by assigning the container to a control group. The image provided here demonstrates how to do this for simple applications. Check out the manual for cgroups to learn more about this feature. If I wanted to restrict what apps and namespaces can do even further, I can set "capabilities", a Linux kernel feature, to restrict the set of system calls processes within the namespace can make. This example here illustrates how this works for simple applications. There are a lot of capabilities that modern Linux kernels offer. See the link below to learn more about them. Then finally, after doing all that, we can run our application. This would be unbelievably inconvenient to do for every application we wanted to containerize. We have haven't even discussed dealing with networking and storage. Fortunately, container runtime simplify all of this work for us. Container runtimes create, manage, and delete containers. They do that by automating everything that we just covered. This way, instead of having to run all these steps to containerize our apps,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/container-runtimes?u=76281980&t=186)** we can do it by simply running Docker run. We will learn how to use Docker run to tweak control groups, capabilities, and other properties later in this course. Container runtimes and container engines are often used interchangeably in conversation. They are similar, but they're not quite the same. This table lists out all the differences between them. While container runtimes are an important component of container engines, engines are responsible for the experience around using containers. This includes activities such as pulling images from container registries, which we'll also learn about later in this course, and providing an API for interacting with containers created by container runtimes. Now that we know more about container runtimes, let's talk about the different kinds of runtimes that exist in the wild.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [OCI and CRI runtimes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/oci-and-cri-runtimes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/oci-and-cri-runtimes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We learned how container runtimes make the process of creating containers dramatically simpler from automatically creating isolated namespaces, to defining the [[Linux]] capabilities apps in those namespaces are, well, capable of using. As you might imagine, lots of opinions about how to execute these steps have arisen. Some runtime maintainers think that containers should not start as root by default. Others think that containers can be created in fewer steps. Many different container runtimes have arisen as a result. Let's take a look at a few of them. There are two quote unquote standards of container runtimes in use today. OCI compatible runtimes and CRI or C-R-I runtimes. OCI is short for the Open Container Initiative. The Open Container Initiative or the OCI is a working group that aims to provide standards for all things containers, like container images and importantly container runtimes. The OCI standard for container runtimes is unsurprisingly called the Runtime Specification or Runtime Spec for short. This specification outlines what a container is and outlines things that container runtimes should do like creating containers and stopping them. It does not dictate how your runtime does those things. Otherwise, this would be a runtime by itself and not a runtime specification. The Runtime Spec is open source and actively maintained. You can read about it on [[GitHub]] here. There are several runtimes
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/oci-and-cri-runtimes?u=76281980&t=94)** that conform to the OCI Runtime Spec. The most popular OCI compliant runtime by far is runc. runc is Docker's original container runtime. Many container runtimes use runc in the background. You can even use it yourself to create containers from scratch like we saw in the last video, though this is a very low level activity that I only recommend doing for learning purposes. Check out this guide from the runc developers if you're interested. Now, we have runc and we have crun. crun is another popular runtime used by Podman, an alternative to Docker. It is maintained by Red Hat and written entirely in C. As a result, crun is smaller than runc and is significantly faster at starting and stopping containers. Another OCI compliant run runtime is youki. youki is a newer OCI compliant runtime written in [[Rust (Programming Language)|Rust]] for similar reasons to crun. Some of you might be thinking, "Hold up a second, Carlos, you said that the OCI Runtime Spec doesn't tell you how to create and stop containers. I have a crazy thought. Don't judge me. Could I use the Runtime Spec to create and stop containers that are actually [[Virtual Machines]]?" Why, yes, yes, you can. There are several OCI compliant runtimes that are used to create containers that are virtualized against another Linux kernel or run entirely on virtual machines. These are called sandboxed container runtimes. They allow you to create containers that are significantly more isolated than non-sandbox containers.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/oci-and-cri-runtimes?u=76281980&t=188)** There are three popular sandboxed container runtimes in the wild today: gVisor, Nabla containers, and Kata containers. gVisor and Nabla create containers against lightweight unikernels that sit between the containers and your machine's actual operating system. This allows you to fine tune what containers can and cannot do even more than using Linux capabilities can. Kata containers takes this concept further by providing an OCI compliant runtime for running containers inside of virtual machines. This is ideal for users who want to benefit from using tools like Docker, but also want the security and isolation that virtual machines provide. All right, well, that covers it for the OCI compatible runtimes. So let's take a closer look at the CRI compatible runtimes. CRI or C-R-I is short for the Container Runtime Interface. This was created and used by the [[Kubernetes]] project. Kubernetes is a platform for running hundreds of thousands of containers across thousands of machines. Kubernetes did this to prevent it from being dependent on any one container runtime or container runtime standard like the OCI Runtime Spec we saw earlier. There are two CRI compatible runtimes in use today: containerd and CRI-O. Containerd is a CRI container runtime that uses runc in the background by default to create OCI compliant containers. Because this is the runtime that Docker uses today, this is by far the most popular
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/oci-and-cri-runtimes?u=76281980&t=280)** CRI compatible runtime at the moment. Even though it uses runc to create containers, containerd supports what are called shims that allow it to use other runtimes like gVisor or kata. CRI-O is a CRI container runtime that provides just enough functionality to provision OCI containers within Kubernetes. It is open source and maintained by Red Hat, Intel. and other large companies. Like containerd, it can use runtimes other than runc to create containers. Whew, that was a lot to cover. In the next video, we'll talk about the Docker engine which uses containerd and several other components to make creating and managing containers easy and fun.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [The Docker Engine](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-docker-engine?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-docker-engine?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've gone into the depths of container run times, let's go up a level and talk about container engines. Container engines work alongside container run times to provide components and tools that make managing containers easy. This can include a number of things from providing additional automation on top of container run times, to including entire domain specific languages, for configuring containers before they start. There are two popular container engines in use today; Docker Engine and Podman. The Docker engine is by far the most popular container engine. It is also the engine that we'll be using for the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of this course. The Docker engine includes the Docker command-line client, a configuration syntax for creating images called the Docker file syntax and an HCTP REST-based API for communicating with container run times. The Docker engine uses the container D runtime by default, but this can be changed relatively easily. Podman is Red Hat's take on a container engine. While it is a default container engine that ships with Red Hat Enterprise [[Linux]], or RHEL, it is functionally equivalent to Docker in almost every use case. There are two key differences though. First, Podman uses Buildah instead of Docker file for creating container images. We'll learn more about container images later in this course. Secondly, Podman uses crun as its default runtime instead of container D. Like the Docker engine,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-docker-engine?u=76281980&t=92)** Podman makes it easy to change this.

#### [Where are Docker's configuration files?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/where-are-docker-s-configuration-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/where-are-docker-s-configuration-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we learned all about container run times and engines, it's finally time to roll up our sleeves and get deep into Docker. Before we begin, let's go over some key Docker files and directories that we'll be referencing throughout the course. In this course, we are going to install Docker "natively" on a lightweight virtual machine running [[Linux]] and interact with it from the Mac. Since Docker will run entirely within this Linux VM, all the files and directories shown here will be for Linux. Quick note for my [[Windows]] users, almost everything we'll do here can be applied for Windows machines. I would recommend to install Docker Desktop in that scenario though. If you haven't done this before, check out my other course, Learning Docker, where we go through this step by step. Okay. Almost all of Docker's data lives in the /var/lib/docker directory. This includes containers, container images and [[Metadata]] used by the Docker client and runtime. The next directory is /var/lib/docker/overlay. This is where container volumes live. Container volumes store data from containers while they're running and we'll learn about them later in this course. I mentioned earlier that the Docker engine exposes an API for interacting with other components like the containers runtime. Similar to other APIs you might have used, the Docker command line client communicates with this API over the network. However, unlike those APIs, communicating to this "network"
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/where-are-docker-s-configuration-files?u=76281980&t=96)** is done through a special file called a Unix socket. A Unix socket is a file that makes it easy for multiple applications on a machine to talk to each other. By default, this Unix socket lives in /var/run/docker.sock. Finally, Docker relies on /etc, or etsy, /docker/daemon.[[JSON]]
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/where-are-docker-s-configuration-files?u=76281980&t=121)** for configuration and other properties like HTTP proxies and runtime configuration. This file might not exist when you first install Docker but the Docker engine looks for it every time it runs.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Linux]] (3), [[Windows]] (2), [[Metadata]] (1), [[JSON]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (17)
> **Env Vars:** api (2), http (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (3)
> **Tools:** docker desktop (1), command line (1)
> **File Paths:** docker/daemon.json (1)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)


### 2. Installing Docker: The Hard Way

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [When Docker Desktop doesn't cut it](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/when-docker-desktop-doesn-t-cut-it?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/when-docker-desktop-doesn-t-cut-it?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are several ways of installing Docker on [[Windows]], [[Linux]], and Mac. The most popular method by far is installing through Docker Desktop. Docker Desktop is Docker's all-in-one solution for doing all things containers from your desktop and through the command line. Desktop includes a really capable graphical interface for interacting with your containers, the command line client, and a small Linux-based virtual machine that powers the Docker engine in the background. If you're interested in learning how to install Docker Desktop on Windows and on the Mac, check out my introductory course on Docker called Learning Docker. However, there are several situations where Docker Desktop is not the most ideal solution for managing containers. First, Docker Desktop can get expensive for large companies. In 2020, Mirantis, Docker's parent company, changed the licensing agreement for Docker Desktop, such that companies with more than 250 employees or $10 million in recurring annual revenues must pay for a per-user subscription. The cheapest subscription available is $9 per user per month for teams with up to 100 people, and $24 per user per month for larger teams. This can get quite expensive for teams that just want to use Docker. Second, Docker Desktop can be tricky to use in remote environments with limited bandwidth. While you could use remote user interface protocols, like [[Microsoft]]'s Remote Desktop, or VNC to access the Docker Desktop UI, this can be slow and/or bandwidth-intensive.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/when-docker-desktop-doesn-t-cut-it?u=76281980&t=93)** This is worsened by the Docker command line client being capable of connecting to remote Docker engines over HTTP. Finally, while Docker Desktop does handle setting up Linux-based [[Virtual Machines]] for hosting the Docker engine, there might be situations where more control over how the virtual machines provision is desired. Engineers using Docker in highly regulated environments might want to be able to use Docker against the VM running Red Hat Enterprise Linux or Oracle Enterprise Linux. Other engineers might want to further harden the virtual machine, running the Docker engine to comply with industry security standards, like US [[NIST]] or ISO 27001. For these reasons, we are going to roll up our sleeves, and learn how to install Docker the hard way. We are going to install Docker on a Linux virtual machine on the Mac, and in an embedded version of Linux on Windows. Afterwards, we'll learn how to connect our Docker client to the version machine, and run a sample container to verify that everything is working. I hope you're excited as I am. Let's go.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Linux]] (7), [[Windows]] (3), [[Virtual Machines]] (2), [[Microsoft]] (1), [[NIST]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (25)
> **Tools:** docker desktop (9), command line (3)
> **Env Vars:** vnc (1), http (1), nist (1), iso (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (3)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

We're going to use a virtualization tool called Lima to create the virtual machine that Docker will run in. Lima is a lightweight virtual machine manager that deploys [[Virtual Machines]] through the QEMU hypervisor. That's Q E M U. QEMU is an open source hypervisor that's lighter weight than VirtualBox or VMware Fusion, while also being compatible with Intel and Apple Silicon Macs. This is the [[GitHub]] project for Lima. Lima is also open source. You can go visit this link to learn more about the project or find documentation on it. Now, the easiest way to install Lima is with the Homebrew Package Manager, a really easy way of installing apps on Macs. So, let's start by installing Homebrew. To begin, I'm going to, on the same browser, go and visit [https://brew.sh](https://brew.sh). The command to install Homebrew is right underneath Install Homebrew. Click on the clipboard emoji, here, to copy the command. Next, let's open the terminal. I'll paste the command that I copied and hit enter to begin installing Homebrew. I'll type in my password when I'm asked to, and then after that, Homebrew will take a few minutes to install. Now that Homebrew's installed, we're ready to install Lima. To do that, I'll simply type in Brew install Lima, then hit enter.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/installing-lima?u=76281980&t=93)** This will install Lima along with the Kemove Virtual Machine Manager or hypervisor. Once done, confirm that Lima is working by simply typing in Lima--help. If your installation was successful, you should get a brief usage guide describing how to use it like we can see here.

First, we're going to open up our browser and go to [https://github.com/lima-vm/lima](https://github.com/lima-vm/lima).

All right, the first section shown here defines a list of URLs pointing the pre-packaged virtual machines, or images, for Lima to use for this machine. Lima will try each image in order until it finds a working URL. For example, it'll try this URL on line 12, then on line 15. Then if that doesn't work, line 17. Then if that doesn't work, line 22. If that doesn't work, it'll just give up. Additionally, because Lima can create machines on both Intel and Apple Silicon Macs, you can use the arch property to denote which of these platforms each image is compatible with. Setting arch to "x86-64" tells Lima that this image will be used for VMs running on Intel Max, like the one that I'm using to record this course. Whereas setting it to "aarch64" indicates that
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-lima?u=76281980&t=119)** it'll be used for VMs running on an ARM based processor, like our Apple Silicon Macs. Let's move onto the next section called mounts, which starts on line 25 here. This property is set to a list of directories on your computer to mount on to the virtual machine. You can also allow the virtual machine to write to a directory, by setting the directory's writeable property to true, like the /tmp/lima example shown here. We will revisit this section when we learn more about container volume mounting later in this course. The next section to look at is the provision section, starting on line 33 here. This section defines a series of scripts to run against a virtual machine after it boots. The second set of scripts is what actually installs Docker. So let's look at it line by line. The first and second lines tell the shell executing the script to expect the Bash script and how to configure it. The third line tells the script to exit the Dockers installed. It does this by simply running the docker command over here on the left. And if that doesn't work, or if Docker wasn't found, it just exits. The next few lines, configure Docker. So that runs as a system's default user on startup. Finally, the last two lines actually install Docker. As you can see, installing Docker on [[Linux]] is quite easy. #### [Installing Windows Subsystem for Linux (WSL)](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/installing-windows-subsystem-for-linux-wsl?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/installing-windows-subsystem-for-linux-wsl?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's learn how to install Docker without Docker Desktop on [[Windows]]. If you're following along on a computer with [[Windows 10]] Build 1904 or later, your installation of Windows already has the ability to run [[Linux]] inside of it. This feature is enabled by the Windows subsystem for Linux, a background service that is able to translate Linux system calls or syscalls into their appropriate Windows counterparts through a lightweight virtual machine. Turning this feature on and installing Linux with it couldn't be easier. Let's do it together. While I'll be using Windows 11, this process is very similar for Windows 10, as well. Begin by clicking on the Start button, then typing in Windows feature. You should see Turn Windows features on or off pop up in the menu. Click on it once it appears. Next, look for the features virtual machine platform and Windows subsystem for Linux. And when you find them, go ahead and check them off. Then click okay. It might take a few minutes for those features to apply. Once they apply, you might be asked to reboot your PC. If so, click on restart now. Once your system reboots, go ahead and log in. Once you're on your desktop, the next thing that we need to do is install a version of Linux. Let's try using [[Powershell]] and Admin mode for this step. To do that, click on the Windows key, then type in terminal. Then right click it and run it as Admin.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/installing-windows-subsystem-for-linux-wsl?u=76281980&t=97)** You'll be asked to elevate your permissions. Click yes when you are. Now your terminal should launch Windows PowerShell by default. If it doesn't, you can click on this dropdown arrow and select Windows PowerShell from the list. Now that we're inside PowerShell, let's type in wsl--update to update our installation of wsl. Now, if there were any updates to install, you would see them being installed here. Once the installations finish, type in wsl--shutdown. This ensures that we have a clean environment for the next step, which is to install a Linux distribution. To find Linux distributions, we'll type in wsl--list--online. This shows us all of the valid distributions that we can install. I'm going to install [[Ubuntu]]. Let's do that by typing in wsl--install--d:ubuntu. Then hit enter. WSL will download and install Ubuntu right after that. This might take a few minutes depending on the speed of your computer and internet connection, so just go get some fresh air or get another coffee and just stand by. > **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/installing-windows-subsystem-for-linux-wsl?u=76281980&t=191)** Or you can do it my favorite way, which is to type in shutdown /r /t 0.

And if you see a shell like we see here, that means the installation has been successful, and you're ready to go.

#### [Visualizing your containers with Portainer](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/visualizing-your-containers-with-portainer?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/visualizing-your-containers-with-portainer?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One convenient feature that Docker Desktop provides is the ability to see and interact with the containers that are running. Fortunately, there are community alternatives that provide much of that same functionality. One of my favorite alternatives is a tool called Portainer. Portainer is a front end for the Docker client. Almost everything that you can do with the Docker command line client can be done within Portainer through a super slick UI. Since Portainer itself runs within a Docker container, quote, unquote, "installing it" couldn't be easier. You can find it at [github.com/portainer/portainer](https://github.com/portainer/portainer). Now to our terminal. One quick note before we begin. We're going to quickly run a Docker command with a lot of flags defined, but don't fret, we'll learn about what each of those flags do and more later in this course. >

### 3. Docker Images Demystified

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What are Docker images?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/what-are-docker-images?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/what-are-docker-images?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, let's recap what we've learned so far. We've learned that containers use [[Linux]] control groups and namespaces to package applications and their dependencies into single portable units. We've also learned that containers are created and managed by container engines that use container runtimes behind the scenes to do all of the heavy lifting. Finally, we saw how to install the Docker Engine "the hard way", but let's revisit namespaces for a second. We saw how to unshare to run a process like a shell in a new namespace. We also saw how container runtimes combine this with the chroot command to make the process think it has access to an entire file system while really only giving it access to some random folder on a disc. We now know how to do this ourselves with random applications, but how do container runtimes do this with entire containers? They do so with container images. You can think of container images as "file systems" for containers packaged into single files. When a container runtime creates a container, it extracts the files and directories from the image, puts them into a random folder, and tells a container, "Here's your root directory. Have fun." If you're familiar with [[Virtual Machines]], container images are very similar to virtual discs in a virtual machine. So with that, let's explore the anatomy of a container image. Almost every container image will be a tar file compressed with gzip. Tar and gzip are just archival formats that are compatible with each other.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/what-are-docker-images?u=76281980&t=94)** Container images are compressed like this to keep their size down as much as possible. Some images might be compressed with other formats like Zstandard or zstd instead of gzip. Going one level deeper, each image will contain one or more "layers". Layers are compressed archives that contain a set of files that make up the container's file system along with some data about itself. These files are combined together to make up the folder that the container is given for its root file system. The last layer in an image usually, but not always, contains instructions for configuring the container. We'll look at this in more detail when we talk about open container initiative images or OCI images later in this course. Finally, each image will contain a manifest at their root levels that provides more information about itself, including the relationship between its layers. This is usually in a [[JSON]] file, but it doesn't have to be. The manifest file is important. The container runtime uses manifests as guides for how layers should be extracted and their containers configured. For this reason, we say that containers are created from container images. This is also why you must provide an image name or path when you create a container.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Linux]] (1), [[Virtual Machines]] (1), [[JSON]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3), tar (2), docker (1)
> **Env Vars:** oci (1)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Diving deeper into Docker images: Part 1](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/diving-deeper-into-docker-images-part-1?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/diving-deeper-into-docker-images-part-1?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's open our terminal and take a closer look into the anatomy of a container image. Your exercise files for this chapter should include an example of the hello world Docker image that we retrieved while testing our Docker installation earlier but with some slight modifications. It should be called hello-world.tar.gz. Make sure that it's there before continuing. You'll also need to have the tar program installed on your computer for this chapter. Check that you have it by running tar --version. If you get a command not found error message, this means that you do not have it installed. If you're on [[Windows]], I recommend using the Windows Subsystem for [[Linux]] feature for this video and installing it through your Linux distribution's package manager. Okay, let's do it. Let's begin by inspecting the contents of hello-world.tar.gz. To do that, I'm going to type tar -t -f dot, which is short for our current directory, slash, then hello-world.tar.gz. If you have the file in your directory, you should be able to just hit Tab and have it auto complete like it did here. Dash t tells tar to list but not extract the contents of this file, which is provided by dash f. So after hitting Enter, you'll see a few files. Specifically you should see a file called manifest.[[JSON]], a file called repositories, and then a few layers, namely two layers.

> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/diving-deeper-into-docker-images-part-1?u=76281980&t=97)** Mine start with 4e5f, and 1fa3. I know that they're layers because they have layer.tar in their names. Finally, you should also see a file with a really long ID that just ends in JSON. In my example, that file is f95b0. Let's start with manifest.json. What I'm going to do now is I'm going to use tar-x which tells tar to extract our file, -O to extract the file to our screen instead of onto our disk, and then I'm going to provide -f and then hello-world.tar.gz like we did last time. And then finally, I'm going to provide the path to our file which is just manifest.json. Now, the reason why I'm doing it this way is because I don't want to extract everything onto the computer. I don't want to spend more space. I just want to look at what's inside of this file. All right, so we can see some stuff here but it's a little bit difficult to read. So what I'm going to do is take this output and feed it into a program called jq. Jq is a really useful tool for slicing and dicing JSON but it also makes it easier to read JSON like this. I'm going to provide a dot and then I'm going to hit Enter and it'll automatically pretty print the output that we saw earlier. If you don't have jq installed, you can install it by typing brew install jq. Okay, let's explain what we're looking at here.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/diving-deeper-into-docker-images-part-1?u=76281980&t=190)** The config file tells the container runtime where the container's configuration is within the image. In this image, the config is located in a JSON file starting with f95b. This file will also be named after the image's ID. RepoTags lists the various names or tags associated with an image. ImageTags give us two important pieces of information: the name of the image, and optionally, the image's version, quote unquote, which is separated by a colon. In this example, the name of the image is called hello-world and the version is v1. Note that this doesn't actually have to be a version. It's just typically used for that purpose. If we didn't specify v1 when we built this image, it would just say Latest here. Finally, we have Layers which simply lists the locations of the layers in our Docker image. As you can see, each layer is yet another compressed image and they also matched the tar files that we saw where we ran tar tf. We can see that down here. Cool. Let's open this config file here. I'm going to go ahead and copy that, and then what I'm going to do is I'm going to use Control + R to go back to my -x command from earlier, but this time, I'm going to remove the manifest.json part of it and instead, paste the thing that I just copied. This is a lot of stuff here.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/diving-deeper-into-docker-images-part-1?u=76281980&t=283)** This is quite busy. We don't need to know all of these properties but I'll go over some of the more important ones. The first important one is the architecture which we can see up here. Architecture specifies the process of architecture that the image was built for. This is also the architecture that containers created from this image will inherit. Since containers virtualize the operating system kernel, the binaries within the container need to match the processor architecture the container will run on. In other words, you can't run containers with binaries compiled for ARM processors like Apple silicon on Intel MAX and vice versa. There is a way around this though, and we'll talk about that later in the course. Container_config describes the configuration of the container that built the last layer of the image. Config, on the other hand, describes the configuration that containers created from this image will inherit. History outlines the commands that the container runtime ran to generate this image. This can be helpful in figuring out how an image was built which can come in handy when inspecting images from unknown sources. Finally, os specifies the operating system containers created from this image need to be running on. This field is really important because as we learned from earlier, you can't run Linux containers on native windows and vice versa. Now, by quote native Windows, I'm talking about the Docker engine that's running on top of the Windows kernel, not the Docker engine that we installed into a Linux distribution within Windows through WSL.

#### [Storage drivers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/storage-drivers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/storage-drivers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've learned how container images work behind the scenes, let's talk about how container run times store their layers on disk. Let's revisit our breakdown of a container image from the last chapter. We learned that each of these layers are compressed as tar files. Since containers, access files from directories not tar files, container run times need to decompress them into directories so that they can get assigned as fake root directories to containers. For a single container, this is pretty straightforward. Let's put our container runtime pads on, and pretend that we're going to create a container that's going to run some kind of an application from within a container image with several layers in it like you can see here. Since the container needs a fake root directory before it can do anything, we'll first decompress the image, and any compressed layers within it into a directory called layers, which you can see here in the middle of the page. Once that's done, we'll use the unshare command to run our application into new name space. Put that name space into a truth that's pointing to our layers directory. Wrap that around some C Group magic and boom, our container is up and running. This works well enough for a single container, but what if we wanted to create tens, hundreds, or even thousands of containers whose images slightly deviate from the one we just used? With our previous process, we would need to create folders for every single container, and decompress our image into every single one of those folders. It would look something like this.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/storage-drivers?u=76281980&t=93)** This would become a huge problem for even slightly large images. Imagine having to wait for a 100 megabyte tar file to decompress every single time you want it to start a container. Docker probably wouldn't be as popular as it is if we had to do that. It would be great if we could create as many containers as we wanted without having to decompress this image every time. Ideally, we could point fake root directories within containers that consume the same container images to the same directory like shown here. Since container images can share layers between each other, it would be even better if we can put each layer in its own directory, and use some kind of a overlay to smash them together into a single directory. Fortunately, container run times are capable of doing this. They do so with storage drivers. Storage drivers define how layers are stored on disk. They're also responsible for how they're represented to containers. In other words, storage drivers are responsible for decompressing layers into a directory structure, and presenting them to a container as a fake route directory. Some container run times refer to storage drivers as graph drivers or snapchatters, but there's no need to worry as they're interchangeable. There are several storage drivers in the wild today. The most popular storage driver by far is the overlay2 storage driver. Let's explain how it works. First, overlay2 decompresses each layer
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/storage-drivers?u=76281980&t=186)** in a container image into the container run times configuration directory. In the docker engine, these are almost always located at slash var, slash lib, slash docker, slash overlay2. The names of each layer might or might not correspond to that layer's ID. Alternatively, shortcuts to these layer directories or symbolic links or SIM links are created within a directory simply called l. That's lowercase l. This is the bottom half of the fake route directory given to a container. Next, the storage driver creates a few directories within each layer directory. The bottommost layer in an image is given two directories, link and diff. The diff directory contains the contents of that layer, whereas link contains an ID that identifies a symbolic link created for it in l or lowercase l. Layers above the bottom-most layer will have two extra directories, lower and merged. Lower points to the idea of the layer above it, whereas merge contains the diffs of its own layer with the layer underneath it. Next, the storage driver creates a work directory. Containers use the work directory to keep track of any new files or directories created by the application it's running that are not in lowercase l. Finally, the storage driver creates what's called overlay mount, that combines the lowercase l, and lower layer directory into a single folder for the container to use.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/storage-drivers?u=76281980&t=279)** Internally, this folder is quote unquote, "Created by the overlay FS file system with the Mount [[Linux]] system." Overlay FS mounts have two components, a lowerdir, which is the bottom box here towards the middle of the screen, and an upperdir, which are all the layers above it or all the directories above that bottom directory. Directories submerge are specified within the lowerdir and are merge into the upperdir to create an entire virtual file system, which is represented by this orange box. Any changes to that file system are made into the upperdir. Now, if a container creates new files that did not exist in any of the container's images, or if any of those new files are deleted, then those changes get made into the upperdir without issue. But what happens when you want to modify or delete a file that's a one of the child layers in the upperdir, but not in any of the lowerdir? That's where stuff gets a little interesting. Because the container thinks that it's writing to a single file system. The file it wants to delete or modify needs to exist, even if that file isn't in the lowerdir. To magically make that happen, overlay FS does a slight-of-hand called a copy-ups to copy the missing file from a lower directory up into the upperdir. Or another hand trick called a whiteout if it's been quote unquote, "Deleted." Now remember, containers can only write to the upperdir, not a layer directory. You can kind of think of a whiteout file
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/storage-drivers?u=76281980&t=371)** like putting up a police line "Do not cross" tape over a crime scene. The file still exists, but the container can't access it. As you can imagine, copy-ups can get really really slow depending on how large the file being copied up is. Even though copy-up operations only happen once specifically to get the file from the lowerdir, this latency can be painful for IO sensitive applications like [[Databases]] or messaging systems. Another big disadvantage with this overlay file system is that any data persisted into it is removed once the container is deleted. Put it another way, you lose everything. you save into a container by default. The easiest ways to work around these limitations is by using either volumes or bind mounts. Volumes are more performant than bind mounts, but bind mounts are much easier to use, and work with. We'll learn more about both of these concepts later in the course.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Linux]] (1), [[Databases]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** tar (3), docker (3), make (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (2)
> **Env Vars:** sim (1)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1)
> **Definitions:** in other words (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Decomposing Docker pull](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/decomposing-docker-pull?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/decomposing-docker-pull?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Since Docker does not show us debug level logs by default we need to tweak Docker's configuration settings a tiny bit first. This is easy to do. First, we'll need to enter the virtual machine that's actually running Docker on our machine. Since I'm recording this from a Mac that's running Docker with Lima I can do that by simply typing limactl shell docker here If you're on [[Windows]] with W S L you can just go into the [[Ubuntu]] Shell within terminal or type dash and you'll be in the same place. If you're running Docker desktop run docker run -it --rm --privileged --pid=host
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/decomposing-docker-pull?u=76281980&t=44)** justincormack/nsenter1 to accomplish the same effect. If you're running Docker inside of a [[Linux]] distribution within Windows. All you need to do is type dash. #### [The anatomy of a Dockerfile](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-anatomy-of-a-dockerfile?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-anatomy-of-a-dockerfile?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Earlier, we learned that container images are composed of one or more layers that are compressed as tar archives and joined together by a union file system. We also saw how OverlayFS creates these layers and mounts them into containers. It would be really, really tedious if we had to create and compress each layer by hand. Automating this process would be ideal, but how could we go about doing that? There are two approaches that I can think of towards potentially automating the creation of each layer. We could write a shell script that uses [[Linux]] built-ins and the tar program to create each layer or we could then make a more human readable version of this by leveraging [[Configuration Management]] tools like [[Ansible]] or Chef to create each layer. Unfortunately, both of these approaches have pretty significant disadvantages. Using scripts to generate each layer would become very complex as we would need to account for all of the potential commands that users might run while creating their layers, as well as a ton of edge cases that might arise. This increased complexity introduces more chances for error as well, which is not ideal. Configuration management definitely simplifies some of this complexity, However, that simplicity comes at the cost of needing to deal with ensuring that the configuration management tool is installed and properly configured. We would also need to do this on every machine that runs our image creation automation, which is less portable not very user friendly, and ironically, just as complex and error-prone as dealing with scripts.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-anatomy-of-a-dockerfile?u=76281980&t=94)** It would be really nice if there was a convenient language for creating our image layers instead of relying on scripts or configuration management. Ideally, this language will make it easy to describe our image and run commands to configure each of the images' layers in a single file. Fortunately, the Docker maintainers have the same wish and made it come true with Dockerfiles. The Dockerfile is a language for creating and describing container images. Dockerfile is the name of the language, as well as the default name of the file that Docker looks for when creating container images. Everything you need to create a container image can be done from a single Dockerfile, which is really cool. Every Dockerfile consists of a series of commands or instructions, each on their own line. The instruction is the first [[Microsoft Word|word]] of the statement. Every other word after the instruction is provided to it as arguments. Let's take a closer look at the first highlighted line, FROM [[Ubuntu]]. In this example, FROM is the command or instruction. Since Ubuntu comes after FROM, it is provided as an argument to FROM. If we had more words after FROM, these too would be treated as arguments. Docker processes Dockerfiles from top to bottom. Almost every command in the Dockerfile is executed in its own temporary container. These are sometimes called intermediate containers. Every Dockerfile starts with the FROM instruction. FROM defines the base image from which your image will be created. This image is linked to your new image and given to the second command in your Dockerfile as is.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-anatomy-of-a-dockerfile?u=76281980&t=190)** The byproduct of the command that ran in that container is saved to a layer. This layer is then added to your new image. Your new image is then given to the intermediate container created by the next command in your Dockerfile. This process repeats until every command in the Dockerfile is processed. Here's an image that demonstrates this process for two commands. Note that some of the Dockerfile commands available like label, expose, and entry point do not run in intermediate containers. Dockerfiles also support comments. These can help make your Dockerfile easy to read. To add a comment, simply start your line with a pound sign. They are not processed by Docker at all. In this chapter, we're going to learn some of the more important Dockerfile commands by building the Dockerfile for our own image step-by-step. We'll also use Docker image and Docker run commands to test our Docker image as we build it. The exercise files for the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the sections in this chapter contain a shell script called app.hs. There should also be a directory called Include that contains a few text files. These will be added into our Docker image to test certain Dockerfile functionality. I've added these exercise files to this video so that you can have a look at them before we create our Docker image. While the Shell scripts are written in Bash, you will not need to know Shell scripting to follow along with this chapter I'll explain any shell scripting nuances that you need to know as we follow along. Have fun!

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Configuration Management]] (4), [[Microsoft Word|Word]] (2), [[Ubuntu]] (2), [[Linux]] (1), [[Ansible]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (9), make (3), tar (2), ansible (1)
> **Exercise Files:** exercise files (2)
> **Tools:** bash (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### [Sourcing other Docker images with FROM](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sourcing-other-docker-images-with-from?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sourcing-other-docker-images-with-from?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, the first Docker file we're going to dive into is the FROM command. FROM specifies the base image, a container image that your new Dockerimage will be created from. The example shown here shows the most common form of the FROM command, the [[Microsoft Word|word]] "FROM" followed by the name of the base image, or "[[Ubuntu]]", in this example. In this form, the latest version of the container image available will be chosen. In our container run times video, we learned that we would need to create a [[Linux]] root file system if we were going to create a container image from scratch. Base images simplify this by allowing us to select an image that already has this root file system and other useful software and configurations included. Behind the scenes, when Docker encounters the FROM statement, it will take the layers of the base image that you provided and puts them underneath the first layer of your new image. Remember, images assorted in reverse order. Pretty cool. Now, "What if I wanted to define my own root file system?", you might be thinking. You can do that too. To create your own base image, simply use FROM scratch. While you can save a lot of space by removing pretty much everything that your app doesn't need, this is an advanced technique that requires some care. Because FROM defines the base image from which your new image will be created, it must be the first line in your Dockerfile. The only exceptions to this are comments and to define variables with the ARG. That's A-R-G or E-N-V or M commands, which we'll learn more about later.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sourcing-other-docker-images-with-from?u=76281980&t=96)** We saw the most common incantation of the FROM command earlier. There are three other ways that you can use FROM as well. You can add a tag after the image to specify a quote, unquote, "version" of the base image that you'd like to use. To do that, simply type a colon after the image name, then provide the tag that you'd like to use. For example, if I wanted to use the kinetic version of Ubuntu, I would write FROM ubunto:kinetic. This technique is called either version pinning or image pinning. It allows you to guarantee that your image will be derived from a specific version of a base image. This can avoid compatibility issues and other pitfalls associated with choosing the latest version of a base image. You can also give the base layer an alias by providing AS then the name of the alias after the base image. This is used when creating multi-stage images, which we'll learn about in a few videos from now. You can name your alias anything you want. Here, for example I'm calling this base image quote "base". This makes it easier to use later in my image pipeline. All right, let's try it. All right, I have opened a terminal here. > **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sourcing-other-docker-images-with-from?u=76281980&t=192)** I'm going to type ls, then hit enter, and by doing that I should see Dockerfile show up here. Do what you'd like. Now I'm going to add a line to start my FROM statement by hitting enter, and then typing in FROM in caps. (keyboard clicks) Now, it doesn't have to be in caps, but I like putting them in caps because it makes the Dockerfile easier to read. Completely up to you though. Next, I'm going to put the base image that I'm going to use. In this case, it's going to be Ubuntu, since we've been using that in our examples. And there it is. We're done for now. #### [Adding and copying files with COPY and ADD](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-and-copying-files-with-copy-and-add?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-and-copying-files-with-copy-and-add?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We often find ourselves wanting to include files and folders into our images. Fortunately, the Docker file language makes this easy to do with the COPY and ADD instructions. COPY copies files and directories within the context provided to Docker image build into a layer within our Docker image. ADD is an older version of the COPY command with two key differences. First, it can automatically decompress tar files from a website into the folder provided and it can automatically download tar files from a URL. Why are there are two commands that do the same thing? Well, to answer that, we need to go back in time a little bit. In 2017, the Docker file maintainers thought that ADD was becoming too large of a command and wanted to use a new command that focused only on copying files into Docker images. However, by this point, Docker was beginning to take the world by storm and many hundreds of thousands of Docker files were using the ADD command. Removing this command would break all of them and make many developers very unhappy. To prevent the almost certainly never ending stream of complaints that would arise from removing ADD, the maintainers chose to create a new and more secure version of ADD called COPY and updated their documentation to recommend that people use COPY instead of ADD instead. In this course, we're going to follow the maintainer's advice and only use COPY in our Docker files. There are three ways of using COPY. We can either copy files into files, which is shown
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-and-copying-files-with-copy-and-add?u=76281980&t=95)** in the first row, copy files into directories, which is shown in the second row, or copy directories into directories, which is shown in the last row. The first argument to COPY is the file or directory within our context to be copied. The second argument is the path inside of the container image that the file or directory in the first argument will be copied into. If the second argument ends in a slash, Docker will assume that it's a directory. The first argument to COPY and ADD also supports wildcards. You can use an asterisk to copy files or directories that start with a specific [[Microsoft Word|word]] or a question mark to replace single characters in a file or directory. For example, let's say that we have a directory in our context called songs. In it, we have 10 files, each named song hyphen dollar x dot mp3 and another 10 songs named song dollar x dot mp3 where dollar x is a number from one to 10. The command COPY song slash song hyphen question mark dot mp3 slash songs would copy all the MP3 files starting with song hyphen into a directory called slash songs in the container image. However, it will not copy the songs without the dash in the title. This is because the question mark only replaces a single character in a file or directory. If we wanted to copy any MP3 file that started with song, we would need to use COPY song slash song star,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-and-copying-files-with-copy-and-add?u=76281980&t=192)** space slash songs. The reason for this is to asterisk copy anything starting with song. Here are two other helpful arguments to COPY and ADD support. You can use the dash dash ch own or chown command to set a user in group on a directory or file copied into [[Linux]]-based container images. This is helpful if the app running within your container image will run as a different user than the user created in its base image. You can also use dash dash link to copy files and directories into a blank layer. We'll explore why this might be helpful when we learn about layer ordering towards the end of this chapter.

#### [Customizing your Docker image with RUN](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/customizing-your-docker-image-with-run?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/customizing-your-docker-image-with-run?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A Docker image would be pretty useless if we could only copy files into it. Fortunately, the RUN Command provides a really flexible way for us to customize and configure our image. The RUN command runs shell commands within temporary containers. These containers are created from image layers generated by the Docker file commands that preceded them. Here are a couple of files to explain what I mean. Right now, our Docker file looks kind of like the first two lines in this image here. It only contains the FROM commands that will link layers within the latest [[Ubuntu]] image to our Docker file, and a COPY command that creates a new layer on top of that, which will contain our exercise files in a directory called /app. Now, let's say we wanted to throw in a RUN command that does something that we want like say RUN stat /app. Because RUN runs shell commands and containers, when Docker sees this line in our Docker file, it'll create a new container that is configured to run something like /bin/sh -c foo. Because RUN runs shell commands in containers, when Docker sees this line in our Docker file, it will create a new container that is configured to run something like what you see in the blue box here, /bin/sh -c. And then in double quotes, stat /app. If we were building a [[Windows]]-based image, it would look like CMD /s/c foo, or whatever the command is, instead.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/customizing-your-docker-image-with-run?u=76281980&t=97)** However, since every container needs an image, where would the image for this container come from? Since every RUN container uses the image of the Docker file command that came before it, it's going to use the image that was generated by the COPY command. That's why we can run stat /app and know that'll work. The image that command will run in will have the /app directory in it already. Now, what would happen if we swapped lines two and three, so that the RUN command comes before the COPY command? Well, since the RUN command will run this command against the container from the Ubuntu image, and that image doesn't have the /app directory in it yet, this command will fail and our Docker build process will stop. Command order matters a lot when creating Docker files. Order can make the difference between a working, and a non-working Docker file. It can also make the difference between a slow Docker build run and a faster one. We'll learn more about layer ordering towards the end of the chapter. There are two ways we can run commands with the RUN instruction. The first way is the shell form, like you can see here, where we run RUN, and then the command. The second way to run it is the exec form shown below. Commands written in shell form run inside of a shell like we saw earlier. This means that you get all the benefits of running in a shell like pipes, chaining, and variable substitution, which matters if we were using the ARG, A-R-G, or ENV, E-N-V, Docker file commands,
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/customizing-your-docker-image-with-run?u=76281980&t=193)** two commands that we'll learn about later. The caveat to this is that the sub-process created for this command will be forked from a shell. This can have some implications around things like using Ctrl+C to exit hung programs, or the way that text is displayed onto your terminal. Commands written in exec form are run directly within the container without a shell. This means that any input sent to the container will go directly to the app that was created by RUN. Now, notice the difference between the formatting here. You can see that commands in shell form have simply the command that we're going to run after the RUN statement. So in the first row, we have RUN, and then immediately we have echo Hello world. Whereas in exec form, our command is surrounded in brackets. And furthermore, each [[Microsoft Word|word]] in our command is separated by a comma. So instead of saying RUN echo Hello world, we say RUN left bracket echo, Hello world end bracket. Shell form and exec form will come into play again later when we learn about the CMD and entry port commands later in this chapter. For now, let's stick to the shell form as that is usually better for commands started by the RUN instruction anyway. So with that, let's go back into our terminal, and give RUN a try. I'm going to open the Docker file again in my Vim Editor. So let's say that we wanted to ensure that the program
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/customizing-your-docker-image-with-run?u=76281980&t=289)** Curl is installed in this image. For those that haven't used it, Curl is a program that can [[Fetch]] webpages, and other web content right from your terminal. It's an extremely useful tool to have in your toolbox. This is a good example of a configuration that RUN is well suited for. Since we're working with an Ubuntu-based image, my RUN command is going to look something like this. I'm going to do RUN then apt
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/customizing-your-docker-image-with-run?u=76281980&t=315)** - y update, then two ampersands, then apt -y install curl. I'm using apt update to ensure that we have the latest information from Ubuntu's Aptitude repositories, and using apt install to install Curl. By default, apt will ask me to confirm that I want to install Curl or update. So I just provided -y in both instances to work around that. Those of you that might have used Ubuntu before might notice that I didn't prepend these commands with sudo. That's S-U-D-O. Why is that? Well, remember, containers run as root by default in Docker. Sudo is used to tell [[Linux]] to run something as root or another user. But since we are root here, we don't need to sudo. All right, now that we've added our RUN statement, let's save and close our Docker file. And now that we've done that, let's go ahead and rebuild our image. The reason for that is the layers from our previous Docker image build runs are cached, and because none of the lines in our Docker file changed, Docker simply pulled those layers in, and just used them again instead of having to rebuild it entirely from scratch. This saves us quite a bit of time when we're developing our Docker files.
