#### [Starting your app with ENTRYPOINT](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-entrypoint?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-entrypoint?u=76281980&t=0)** - Okay, so we defined our base image and copied our mysterious app into this container image. There's only one thing left to do. We got to make this image run our app. Okay, we actually have a few other things to do after that but getting our image to run our app is pretty important. There are two Dockerfile commands that can execute stuff. Entrypoint and CMD. Let's go ahead and start with entrypoint. Entrypoint, like the name implies, configures the container image to run an application when a container is created from it. If you recall from earlier, our base image is configured to have containers run bash on startup. Consequently, containers created from our image will do the same thing. This will change once we define an entrypoint command, which we'll do in a few minutes, so get excited about that. Like the run command that we saw earlier, entrypoint takes two [[Forms]]. The shell form and the exec form. They work exactly like the shell and exec forms that we saw then. However, unlike run, the form that you choose with your entrypoint affects how your application behaves. If your entrypoint uses the shell form, then your program will be created as a child of CMD or SH. This means that any signals that are sent to your application from the terminal, like control C, the interrupt signal or control Z or Zed, the backgrounding signal are caught by the shell instead of your program. And any code in your application that runs whenever these events occur will not execute.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-entrypoint?u=76281980&t=94)** If you've ever started a containerized app from an image in Docker hub and can't forcefully stop it with control Z, this is most likely the reason. Another disadvantage inherent to shell form is that arguments passed into your program through Docker run will get sent to the shell instead of your program. In most cases, this isn't what you want. We'll see an example of this when we update our Dockerfile. There is one advantage of using the shell form that can be useful for certain kinds of Dockerfiles. Since your application is executed by the shell, you can leverage the shell's built-in features directly from your Dockerfile, like expanding environment variables or pipes. However, I don't actually recommend doing this in your Dockerfiles. It makes them harder to read and more complex, as most operators expect complex logic to exist in separate scripts instead of Dockerfiles. A better way of leveraging sell features while allowing your app to receive signals and arguments is to use the entrypoint script pattern. The entrypoint script pattern simply takes all of the logic that you put in your Dockerfile in shell form and puts it into its own script that is invoked with the exec form. This slide demonstrates a simple example of the entrypoint script pattern in action. Entrypoint scripts make your Dockerfile easier to read and debug without removing control from your app like the shell form does. The exec form solves all of the problems inherent to shell form by making your application the top level process inside of your container. In other words,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-entrypoint?u=76281980&t=186)** when Docker creates a container from your image, your application will be the first thing it launches. Every signal and argument provided to it will be shipped directly into your program, which is almost always what you want. All right, I think that's enough theory on entrypoint, so let's open a terminal and put this knowledge to work. All right, so let's begin by using LS to list the contents of this exercise folder. You should see a file called app dot SH in here. If you do, then congratulations, you found our entrypoint. So, let's open the Dockerfile in our editor and make that happen. Adding our entry point is simple. We're going to start with the shell form. To do that, I'm going to add a new line or if you already have a new line then you don't need to add it. And then I'm going to type entrypoint in the caps, followed by the name of our app. Now, since we're copying our app and it's associated files into the slash app folder within the container, we need to use the app's absolute path from the container images perspective. In this case, that's going to be slash app, slash app dot SH. That's it. So let's save our file, close our editor, and then rerun the same Docker image bill command from earlier. Because we changed our Dockerfile, the files in our context changed, and because that happened, when we ran the copy command in our Dockerfile, which we can see here, it changed all the layers underneath it or on top of it because they're stored in reverse order. So because of that, it had to run this run command again,
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-entrypoint?u=76281980&t=282)** which is why it was a little slower to run than when we ran it the last time. Okay, now that the image has been updated, let's create a new container from it with Docker run. So Docker run dash dash RM and then the topmost layer, which is 85b0. Very cool. As we can see here, our app simply tells us the time, along with some stats about the container that it's running in. The first thing that we can see here is that the date that we're seeing is fetched from the internet. This is where a curl comes into play. If we didn't run our run command this probably would not work. The next thing we can see is a list of processes running within the container. We can clearly see that the process running our app was forked from the process running our shell. Because our app happens to be a bash script, SH slash bin slash SH, launched bash and fed the path to our script as an argument. We also know that our script is a child of the SH process because our script is running here, it's process ID or PID is seven and its parent process ID is one. And as we know, when we run a command in Docker with the shell form slash bin slash SH will have a PID of one instead of the app that we actually wanted to run. Finally, the app is telling us that no arguments were provided to it. That's expected as we didn't provide any. All right, cool. So, we learned earlier that arguments to entrypoint written in shell form provided to the shell, not the app,
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-entrypoint?u=76281980&t=379)** and we've already seen that here. So what does that mean in practice? Well, let's run our Docker run command earlier, but with a slight change. Here we're going to add an argument, like dash dash argument. Now, if I press enter, we can see that even though we provided an argument to app dot SH and SH clearly received it, it wasn't forwarded to our application because it's still saying that we didn't receive any arguments and it's not showing up in the list of processes here. Clearly that's not what we wanted. Since we know that expressing our entrypoint in exec form fixes this, let's go back into our Dockerfile and make that change. We know that exec form is adjacent array from what we saw earlier, with the first [[Microsoft Word|word]] being the program and every other word after it being arguments to our program. So what I'm going to do is delete everything after entrypoint with D, then dollar sign. And then I'm going to place open bracket, and then in double quotes slash app slash app dot SH, and then wrap it in double quotes, and then a closed bracket here. That's it. Very straightforward. So I'm going to press WQ to exit and then I'm going to build my image again. Actually, hold on, let's stop here. It's really inconvenient to have to remember IDs like we've been doing, like, Docker run 85b0, and et cetera. That's really inconvenient,

#### ["Starting" your app with CMD](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-cmd?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-cmd?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So now that we've explored the entry point command, let's turn our attention over to CMD. CMD is very similar to entry point. You can use it to configure containers to run an application on startup... Sort of. Let me clarify. CMD's behavior is dependent on two things, whether an entry point is provided or not, and whether CMD is written in shell or exec form. Assuming our entry point and CMD are written in exec form, that yields four combinations between entry point and CMD. Let's take a look. The first combination is when CMD is provided, but entry point is not. This is similar to the example we saw with [[Ubuntu]] base image. In this combination, containers will simply launch the CMD with the application as its top level process. You usually won't see this pattern as it almost always makes sense to just use an entry point. Next is the most popular combination, both entry point and CMD are defined. In this case, everything provided to CMD will be fed into the entry point as arguments. In this example here, dash dash argument is fed into slash app slash app dot SH. Our app dot SH app will run as the top level process in this container since entry point is written in exec form, but it will receive dash dash argument as a default argument to this entry point. Next is a combination we saw in the last video. An entry point is provided without a CMD.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-cmd?u=76281980&t=93)** But since we already saw what happens there, I'm just going to skip over that. Finally, there's the nill case, a Docker file with no CMD or entry point provided. This is where things get a little interesting. If the base image from which the image we're creating has an entry point or CMD defined, it will simply inherit it. This is the behavior we saw before we learned about entry points, as the Ubuntu base image has a CMD command in it that launches Bash by default. If our image is being written from scratch, however, your images will fall back onto Docker's default entry point, slash bin slash SH dash C, for [[Linux]] containers. Or CMD slash S slash C for [[Windows]] containers. What about shell form, you might be asking. Good question. Here's the same table, but with every entry point in CMD replaced with shell form. You might notice that the effects are literally the same regardless of the combination here. Why is that? Well, as it happens, if an entry point is written in shell form, then it doesn't receive any arguments at all from CMD instructions. Yes, folks, we've found yet another hidden disadvantage of using shell form. In the first combination, since we didn't provide an entry point here and shell form always sends whatever it gets to the default shell, its behavior is the same here, too. Remember, unless you have a really good reason, you're almost always better off writing your entry points and CMDs in exec form. You'll almost always want to use the CMD command to
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-cmd?u=76281980&t=191)** provide default arguments to entry point. This can be useful if say, an image for an app that you're writing needs to have a certain set of flags passed onto it by default whenever users run docker run against it. The nice thing about CMD arguments here, is that they're overridden by whatever the user provides via Docker run. This way you don't have to worry about them being set in stone. But what if I changed argument to whatever, literally whatever. Let's look at the difference. As you can see, when I ran this the first time, dash dash argument was provided, which we expected, but when we ran it the second time, it got replaced with dash dash whatever. That's because, going back to our docker file, because CMD sets a default argument here, we can override it when we provide a different argument in docker run. One more experiment. What if we got rid of this, and instead put dash dash argument directly into our entry point? Well, if we build it again, and then run our program with dash dash whatever, we can see that dash dash whatever was provided
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/starting-your-app-with-cmd?u=76281980&t=380)** to our program along with dash dash argument. Why is this? Going back to our Docker file. What we did here, is we actually made dash dash argument part of the app that containers will run when they're created from this image. What this means is that we can't override it like we did when we type dash dash whatever before. Instead, those arguments get appended into our program, like additional arguments. This is why it's really important to know the difference between entry point and CMD. The last thing you want is for your applications to run defaults like this that your users can't easily change.

#### [Adding variables with ENV and ARG](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Carlos] So far, we've seen how Dockerfiles can be used to create container images from other parents with the from command. We've also seen how to use the run command to configure Dockerfiles to run commands that customize our container images. Finally, we now know how to specify a default command to run on container startup with the entry point command and provide some default arguments for it with the CMD command. Let's revisit our Dockerfile up to this point. At the moment, our images entry point is currently set to our app, /app/app.sh. Because we have a CMD set to --argument line five, we're providing the default argument of --argument to this app as well. However, what if we wanted to allow users to change the default argument to something else, like say --option. What if we also wanted to change the version of cURL that gets installed when we build our image? Now, we could override the entry point and CMD in our image with docker run, such that it installs a version of cURL that we want before running our app. However, that's quite user unfriendly, especially if we wanted to use the same version of cURL every time. It would be much cooler if we could use variables to change our images configuration on build time. Fortunately, we can. Let's learn about build arguments and environment variables. Let's start with build arguments. We can define build arguments with the ARG instruction.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980&t=95)** Build arguments are variables that you provide to Docker build. This here is an example of ARG in action. Let's say we wanted to allow users to change the version of cURL that installs when the image is built. In this example, we've implemented this capability by creating a build ARG called curl_bin on line three. We've then altered our run command so that instead of installing cURL like we were doing before we're installing whatever curl_bin is set too. We can also see that we can reference ARG variables by putting a dollar sign behind them like we're doing here. This is important. You must use the --build-arg flag during docker build to set variables defined with the ARG command. The image here shows that an example of that in action. Docker build won't fail if you forget to provide
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980&t=149)** - -build-arg, but your image might behave differently than you expected. Here's an example of that. I ran docker build against a modified Dockerfile you saw in the last slide. As you can see from the image here the image was built successfully. However, we got a very small message in the logs telling us that nothing was installed. Given that it's a single line in a CL logs we probably wouldn't think anything of it until we tried to start a container from this image. As you can see, that line mattered. Nothing was installed, not even cURL. Consequently, our image is telling us that we need to install cURL for the app to work. Not quite what we expected. Let's put build arguments aside for a second and talk about setting environment variables. The ENV keyword defines environment variables that containers started from an image should receive. The image here provides an example of that. Instead of using a build argument to specify the version of cURL that we want we're making it an environment variable instead. We change that by changing ARG to ENV here. We can also see that we can conveniently reference the environment variable in the same way that we did when it was just ARG. You might have noticed that the examples of ENV and ARG that I provided were set to values already. Just like variables in a programming language you can send ENV and ARG variables to default values. This is useful for situations where you want images to be built with a certain set of defaults while still allowing users to change them
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980&t=242)** when they run docker build. Pretty handy. You might be wondering, "Hmm, ENV and ARG look and behave kind of alike, Carlos. What's the catch? Is Docker being duplicative for historical reasons again?" Actually no. ENV and ARG behaved quite differently in two key ways. The first key difference between them is that ENV variables are defined for every container started from an image. Whereas ARG variables are only used during runtime. This makes ENV variables great for providing default configurations for apps while still allowing users to change them when they start containers with docker run. Speaking of docker run, another big difference between them is that ARGs are variables that are set at build time whereas ENVs are set at run time. Put another way, you cannot override ENV variables through docker build. The only way you can override environment variables is by using the -e or the --env flag with docker run, which we'll see an example of soon. ENV and ARG share two properties. First, you can only use ENV and ARG variables within run instructions in a Dockerfile. This means that trying to use an ENV or an ARG within your entry point will not work. When you do this, it's important to note that the ENV and ARG you want to use in your run command must come before that run command. Remember, ENVs and ARGs create new layers that will be placed on top of your image in order. If you try to run a run command
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980&t=334)** in a layer that doesn't have your ENV or ARG layers behind it, then the run command won't know that they exist. Let's put all this theory into practice and update our Dockerfile. Okay. I want to be able to change the version of cURL that gets installed when I build my image. We can use an ARG or an ENV variable to do this. So let's start with an ARG variable. Since we're installing cURL, let's turn on line numbers. Since we're installing cURL line four we want our ARG statement to come before that happens. To do that, I'm going to add a new line above line four and then put ARG in a variable name. Let's call it curl_bin. Next, we'll need to replace the hardcoded version of cURL we're installing with our ARG. Since we referenced ARGs with the dollar sign followed by the name of the variable I'm going to go into line five and then replace cURL with $curl_bin. Finally, I still want users to be able to install whatever the latest version of cURL is that comes with our [[Ubuntu]] base image. To do that, we need to set a default value for the ARG we just created. I'm going to go back to our new line four and put an equal sign after curl_bin and just curl after that in quotes. That's it. Let's close, rebuild, and rerun. And it works exactly the same as it did before, but waits. We can change curl_bin with the build ARG flag, right? So what happens if we go back up to docker build and change that to some invalid version?
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980&t=430)** Well, to do that we can use --build-arg and then we'll put curl_bin and here followed by an equal sign in some value. Let's make it something that doesn't work like invalid. Now remember, this has to be before the dot or at any point before the dot, but it can't be after the dot. So let's go ahead and hit enter and see what happens. As we can see, our default curl_bin got overrated by the invalid package that we provided, but since invalid doesn't exist, this calls are build to fail. Let's try one more thing before we change our ARG to an ENV. I'm going to go back to our docker run command, but I'm going to change this up a little bit. I want to demonstrate that this container will not know anything about curl_bin. To do that, we need to launch a shell instead of the app we've been working with so far. To do that, we need to override the entry point. We can do that by putting --entrypoint after the rm flag. Next, I'm going to put sh here since I want our default command to just open a shell. Next, I'll move my [[Cursor]] to the end of this command with control + E and put the arguments I want to give sh. Since I want to echo the value of $curl_bin specifically I'm going to put -c, and in single quotes I'm going to put echo $curl_bin. The single quotes are important here as we want to make sure that curl_bin gets expanded within the container not within our own shell. So this looks good. Let's go ahead and run it. Oops, I made a small mistake.
>
> **[8:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/adding-variables-with-env-and-arg?u=76281980&t=522)** I need to remove dash dash argument. As we expected we got nothing here. I expect this since we know that ARGs are not provided to containers, ENVs are. Let's see if we get a different result after changing our ARG to an ENV. I'm going to go back to our Dockerfile and I'm going to change ARG to ENV. Nothing big. That's all we need to do. So I'm going to go ahead and write and then quit it. Then I'm going to build again, but without the build-arg this time. What happens if we do a docker run here? Works exactly the same as before. But what happens if we repeat our echo test from earlier? Let's find out. Look at that. Very interesting. We got the default value put into our Dockerfile back instead of nothing. This proves that ENVs propagate down to containers like we expected them to.

#### [Other helpful Dockerfile commands](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/other-helpful-dockerfile-commands?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/other-helpful-dockerfile-commands?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Now that we've covered the major Dockerfile commands, let's look at some other commands you might find useful while authoring your Dockerfiles. I'm only going to cover the Dockerfile commands that I've found useful in the hundreds of Dockerfiles I've created over the years. If you're interested in learning about other commands that I haven't covered here, or to dig deeper into any of the commands we have covered, I encourage you to check out the Dockerfile reference page at the link shown here. This page is full of the nitty-gritty details of every command supported by the Dockerfile language. It also includes languages that are supported by the newer Docker image builder, BuildKit. BuildKit is Dockerfile's newest image building engine. It supports all the commands we've seen here as well as a few new ones. It also has better support for parallelized build and image building. Visit this link to learn more about BuildKit. All right back to the commands. The first useful command we'll look at is the LABEL command. LABEL allows you to document your images by adding helpful [[Metadata]] to them. Labels accept a single argument: two words joined together by an equal sign. This is also called a key-value pair. The most popular LABEL you'll see in Docker images is the maintainer label like the one shown here. This is typically used to give users an email address to contact if they run into any issues with the image. You might see some older Dockerfiles use the maintainer keyword for this purpose. The Dockerfile maintainers deprecated this command in 2018. Labels appear more easily when inspecting images with a docker image inspect command, and are more accommodating
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/other-helpful-dockerfile-commands?u=76281980&t=93)** to future upgrades to the language. Use the maintainer label instead. There aren't any reserve labels so you can create whatever labels you want, and as many as you want. The next command I like to use often is the WORKDIR command. WORKDIR allows you to configure a working directory for RUN commands within your Dockerfile file, and/or containers created from your image. I find WORKDIR really useful for executing multiple RUN commands that rely on the same directory. This prevents me from repeating myself when I write my RUN commands. Like LABEL, you can use WORKDIR as many times as you want. RUN commands will use whichever WORKDIR you set last, or the root directory, which is just slash the default WORKDIR. Note that the last WORKDIR you set is the one that containers will use whenever they are created from your image. Here's an example of this. On line seven, I set the WORKDIR to the root directory. I then tell Docker to print my current working directory with the pwd command on line eight, and then modified our entry point to use a relative path on lines 9 and 10. After rebuilding the image, we can see that we got our root directory back after running the first pwd command and /app when we create a container from this image. Another really useful Dockerfile command is the USER command. USER allows you to change the [[Linux]] or [[Windows]] user used by RUN commands and/or containers created from your image. You can think of USER as WORKDIR but for [[User Management]]. If your image will create Linux containers,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/other-helpful-dockerfile-commands?u=76281980&t=187)** you can use numeric user IDs or usernames. This can be useful in situations where a specific user ID is expected like containers that will access volume mounted files permission to specific UIDs. Like WORKDIR, you can set USER multiple times throughout your Dockerfile as well as between RUN commands. Also like the RUN command, the last USER command will specify the default USER containers created from your image will run your app as. I use USER a lot for a single reason, it prevents containers from running as root by default. Besides making your security teams happy, this avoids a significant number of security concerns that arise from running Docker and production environments. However, USER does not prevent users from using other users in their containers. Say that seven times fast. The default USER can be overridden by providing the dash dash user command line flag to docker run. You can also create users while building your Dockerfile, and then use the USER keyword to ensure that the containers use that user. An example of this technique is shown here. On line seven, I use the useradd command to create a new user called newuser with a default password supersecret. I then make it the default user on line nine, and after rebuilding the image, I confirm that containers created from this image use the newuser by overriding the entry point with the whoami command. The final command I want to talk about
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/other-helpful-dockerfile-commands?u=76281980&t=281)** is the EXPOSE keyword. This helpful command documents network ports that containers created from your image should expose at runtime. The only argument it accepts is the port number containers should expose. In our example shown here, we're telling users to expose port 8080 when they create containers from this image. By default, EXPOSE assumes TCP ports. It's really important to know that EXPOSE does not automatically expose ports. Containers created from this image here will not automatically have TCP port 8080 exposed when they are created. You will still need to use the appropriate command line flags to make that happen, and we'll learn more about container networking in a few chapters to learn how to do that. And that's it. That's the fundamentals of the Dockerfile language. Next, we'll talk about supercharging your Dockerfiles with multi-stage builds and optimized layer ordering. Stay tuned.

#### [Multi-stage builds](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we know the basics of writing Dockerfiles, let's spend the next few sections learning some tips and tricks for making your images even better. The first feature we'll learn about is multi-stage builds. We know from our chapter on container images that container images can take up space, especially if they have a lot of layers. We've never really considered the size of our image, as we've been focused on getting our app working. If you run docker images my-image, like I did here, we can see that the image is actually quite large. Even though our app is really small, the [[Ubuntu]]-based image includes a lot of tools and utilities. These add up. I was curious about how much bigger our image is relative to our app, so I wrote a small script called size_diff.sh to compare the two. It's included in your exercise files, if you want to play around with it. As it happens our final image is almost 135,000 times bigger than our app. That's quite the difference. As it happens, our app retrieves the date, by using Curl, to query some information from [[Google]], unless we specify a file called /app/include/date.txt. How can we use this information to slim down our image? We would need to do something like this image demonstrates. This approach is called the builder pattern and it works something like this. First, we would need to create two Dockerfiles; one that is a Ubuntu-based, that downloads and runs Curl to generate this file, and another Dockerfile that is based on a smaller base image that runs our app with this file included in it. Because we have two Dockerfiles in this situation,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980&t=96)** we would need to run docker build twice. However, the second docker build will fail, since the date.txt file lives inside of the first image. To work around this, the next thing we would need to do is use the docker create command to create a temporary container that simply copies the date.txt file from the first image onto our computer. Finally, we would need to run our second docker build command. That command's context will receive the date.txt file we created in the first step, producing a final image with the working app that is not as large. Whew, that's a lot of steps. Fortunately, there are a number of disadvantages with this pattern. The first disadvantage is having to maintain two or more Dockerfiles. Keeping track of all these Dockerfiles to produce smaller images shifts your administrative burden from keeping your image size down to wrangling a ton of Dockerfiles. That's not fun. You also saw that the builder pattern involves a lot of steps, certainly more steps than running docker build. Managing and automating these commands adds additional administrative overhead and introduces additional opportunities for human error. Finally, the command shown previously leave a lot of stuff behind. Specifically, we left behind that temporary container as we didn't run docker rm after we were done, as well as the first image that we aren't going to end up using. All of this takes up disk space and further increases overhead, as we would have to probably automate making sure that all of those temporary resources get deleted. The solution to these pain points
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980&t=190)** is using multi-stage builds. Multi-stage builds allow you to create intermediate images that help you generate smaller final images all in a single Dockerfile. Let's take a look at what our builder pattern would look like with multi-stage builds. This is what the commands we saw before would look like as a Dockerfile. As you can see, we've made a few changes and introduced some new concepts here. Let's dig right into them. The first feature that the multi-stage build allows is the ability to use multiple base images. Put another way, multi-stage builds allow you to use the FROM command multiple times. Each group of commands underneath the base image is called a stage. While each stage in a multi-stage build produces a temporary image, the last stage is the stage used to officially create your image. Stages are a zero index, which means that you count them from 0 instead of 1. For example, the first stage is called Stage 0 here and then the second one is called Stage 1. Multi-stage builds allow you to copy files, or directories, between stages. This enables you to create images that only have everything that your app needs to run and nothing else. This is done with the good old copy command, but with an extra twist. Here, you can use the --from flag to specify the stage that contains your file. You can either use the index of the stage, like stage 0, shown here, or you can use their alias. You can name each stage with a multi-stage build. This way you don't ever have to remember indices,
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980&t=283)** which is good for people like me, since I lose track of everything. In our example, instead of remembering that our first group is stage 0 and the second is stage 1, we're just calling the first stage base and the second stage app. This way, we can simply write COPY --from=base instead of from 0, like we did before. Much easier to remember. Okay, so we've been looking at this modified Dockerfile for a few slides, but we really didn't do it justice. Let's do that now and put all these concepts together. The modified Dockerfile explains this further. The first stage on top is based on the Ubuntu base image. Its purpose is to [[Fetch]] the date from [google.com](https://google.com) with Curl and store it as the file that our app is looking for. Notice that the name of the file we're using to store the results from Curl isn't date.txt inside of app/include. Also notice that we aren't copying the app in this stage. This demonstrates the power of the multi-stage build because we only need to copy our app's directory in the app stage of our multi-stage Dockerfile, we don't have to do it in every stage. Additionally, we can see that I'm only using the base stage to grab a single file, the date.txt file, specifically. Since we're using the copy command to do that, we can name it whatever we want in the base stage. Lastly, the app stage, beneath it, is based on the alpine3.16 tag of the bash image. I'm basing app on this instead of a Ubuntu because it's based on Alpine [[Linux]], which is much, much smaller than Ubuntu. This is okay here, since our app
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980&t=375)** doesn't even need much from Linux, other than the ability to run bash, print our file and run some commands. The [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the stage looks very similar to what we've seen. We copy the app, set our entrypoint to have containers start the app automatically and then use CMD to provide a default argument to it. There are two other cool features to multi-stage builds. First, you can actually copy files or directories from images that aren't in your Dockerfile. We can see that here, on line 18. Instead of copying from any of the stages in our Dockerfile, I'm copying this file from some other image, an image I created separately. This can be a really neat way of grabbing tools, or files from your image, without having to install Curl or wget, and keeping tracks of your URLs and stuff. Another cool multi-stage build feature is that you can actually reuse stages as many times as you want. Each stage descends from the image that was created by the stage that's referenced by FROM. Here, we're reusing the base stage in the second and third stages to simply echo a statement. This can be really convenient to prevent repeating yourself. The official Docker documentation on multi-stage builds uses this to build two C++ plus apps from two source files. This keeps your intermediate stages clean. You might be thinking, this is all really cool, Carlos, but can't I just use Alpine for my whole Dockerfile instead of having to worry about stages and stuff? That's a valid question. We very well could do that. However, there are a number of benefits that multi-stage builds bring that are hard to reproduce
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980&t=469)** with even the most optimized Dockerfiles. First, because your final images can have exactly what your app needs to run, they can be really, really small. If you recall from an earlier video, we talked about creating base images from scratch using FROM scratch at the top of your Dockerfiles. Creating a base image from scratch and building it up enough to build and run your app can be difficult. However, it is much, much easier to create a stage that builds your app in a bigger intermediate container and copy the final result into a scratch image. This makes your image not much bigger than your applications, while keeping them completely self-contained, which is really cool. Additionally, multi-stage builds can be much faster than regular Dockerfiles. You might have noticed that we had to wait a few seconds for Curl to install every time we ran docker build. This was because the COPY command always came before RUN and because we were making changes to our Docker file, that layer always changed. This caused layers underneath it to need rebuilding. We avoid this completely with multi-stage builds. Since we only copy our app in the app stage, where we don't run any commands, our RUN layer doesn't need to be rebuilt every time we change our app or Dockerfile, saving us a lot of time. Finally, the biggest reason by far to use multi-stage builds is for security. By removing unnecessary image layers and operating system tools and libraries, we can produce really, really secure images that have fewer opportunities for surfacing vulnerabilities. Your exercise files contain an example
>
> **[9:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-stage-builds?u=76281980&t=565)** of a really optimized version of the Dockerfile we've been using here, called tiny.Dockerfile. It uses a very thin version of BusyBox to run our app. Try building more images from this Dockerfile if you're interested in exploring multi-stage builds some more. As you can see, it produces a 13 megabyte image instead of a 100 megabyte image, like the one we've been building. While that's still a lot bigger than our 876 byte program, it's a huge improvement. Can you make it smaller? Give it a shot.

>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Ubuntu]] (5), [[Google]] (2), [[Linux]] (2), [[Fetch]] (1), [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (10), curl (6), rm (1), wget (1), make (1)
> **File Paths:** date.txt (5), size_diff.sh (1), app/include/date.txt (1), tiny.dockerfile (1)
> **Env Vars:** copy (2), run (2), cmd (1)
> **Definitions:** is called (4), is a  (1)
> **Tools:** bash (2)
> **Exercise Files:** exercise files (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), similar to (1)

#### [Multi-platform images](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So we know that containers can only run on the operating system kernels that their images were built for. Put another way, we can only run [[Linux]] containers on Linux-based machines, and [[Windows]] containers on Windows-based machines. However, there's another limitation within this limitation that we haven't talked about. By default, container images are also processor instruction set specific. Let me clarify what I mean here. I'm recording this on an Intel Mac. We can confirm that by running uname -a, and checking the end of the line. Since it says X86_64 here, we know that we're running on an Intel Mac. Consequently, containers that we start on this system will be created from X86-based container images. We can confirm this easily by re-running the uname command within an Alpine container, which we can do by doing docker run -- rm alpine uname -a. And there we have it. It has X86_64 at the end of it as well, just like our Mac. However, there are many other processor instructions sets out there, Arm is a popular example. Arm-based processors like Apple Silicon, AMD Ryzen, [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]]'s Ampere CPU, and AWS's Graviton CPU are becoming increasingly popular. They usually consume significantly lower power, and in the case of Apple Silicon processors, are significantly faster than their Intel counterparts as well. What does this mean for containerized applications, though? Let's say that we wanted to try running the ARM64 version
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=94)** of the Alpine Docker image on our Intel Mac. To do that, I'm going to go back to our Docker run command, and before alpine, I'm going to add another flag call --platform. This tells Docker to use an image built for a different processor type than the one we're on. I'm going to put linux/arm64 here so that Docker retrieves the ARM version of the Alpine image. Let's hit enter and see what happens. As we can see, we're repulling this image because we don't have the ARM variant, we have the Intel variant, and it failed. It failed with this error specifically. You might have a number of questions upon seeing this, like, "If we can only create containers from processor specific images, how are you able to create an ARM container?", or, "What's an exec format error?", or, "Why are you even showing us this?" Let's answer all of those questions. Processors are what makes a computer tick. Amongst other things, they're responsible for processing machine code and performing operations on them. The way these operations are performed are done through a list of commands called an instruction set, or an instructions set architecture, shortened to an ISA. Instruction sets define things like add these two numbers together, or store this value somewhere temporary for future reference. Different processors have different instruction sets. This allows companies to build processors that focus on different things like super fast math calculations or power efficiency. While X86 and the ARM-based variants
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=189)** are the most popular ISAs in use today, there are many other instruction sets out there. Every program that you use gets compiled into machine code that can be processed by one of these instruction sets. However, instruction sets are not interchangeable. ARM-based processors cannot understand programs written for S86, and vice versa. This is why we're getting an exec format error here. In this case, this is Linux's way of telling us, "Hey, you gave me a program in machine code I don't understand," that program being uname in this case. This was a huge problem for anyone using ARM-based processors in AWS or Azure, or people like me who decided to buy an Apple Silicon MacBook before Docker was ready. Because almost every container image in the Docker hub was created for X86 processors, Docker was basically unusable for anyone with these computers. This was obviously a large problem for Docker as well, as ARM was only becoming more popular. Creating VMs that emulate other processors is not ideal, as this is slow and power hungry. Fortunately, Docker has a really clever way of dealing with this problem. We learned in the container images section that images are composed of several layers, and a manifest that links them together. I actually left something out in this diagram. Images can also contain a component called an image index or a manifest list. Image manifest allows you to map image manifest to processor types, and even operating systems.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=281)** This allows you to tell Docker, "Use these images if we're on an X86 system, but use these images if we're on an ARM-based system." The manifest list is optional. If one is not defined, Docker will assume that the image's manifest works with whatever processor your container is created from. Additionally, due to a technical limitation with how manifests lists work, the images within this list must exist in an image registry like Docker Hub. We will learn how to create multi-platform images in this video, but we will cover putting them together with a manifest list and pushing them into a registry in Chapter Nine. You might be wondering, "How do I build container images for processors I don't have?", that's a great question. When Linux executes an application, one of the things it does is looks for a program that can understand the machine code it was compiled in. These programs are called binary handlers, and they register themselves with a built-in Linux program called binfmt, or binary format. A group of developers cleverly discovered that they could create a binary handler that runs applications within an emulator called QEMU that can emulate other processor architectures. Their work turned into a project called qemu-user-static which is a program that we'll be using to solve this particular problem. qemu-user-static, or QUS, or qus, enables you to use QEMU, an open source emulator and hypervisor, to run applications built for other processor instruction sets. In our case, this allows Docker to emulate
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=375)** many different processor types while processing run commands during image builds. As a result, we can use qus to create multiple representations of a single image that we then link together with an image manifest. Let's go back into our terminal and try it out. Let's say we wanted to make our My Image image compatible with both X86-based systems and ARM-based systems. Since we're on an Intel system, we'll need to use qus to configure binfmt in our VM to pass ARM applications to QEMU. The nice thing about qus is that we can do this in Docker. I'm going to type docker run to start up our command. Since we'll be modifying files in our system directly, we'll need to add a special flag called --privileged next. This tells Docker to configure our container so that they can use our host's devices and files. As you can imagine, this is usually super dangerous. Use this flag with caution. We'll also cover what this flag does exactly in a few videos from now. All right, moving on. We won't need this container when we're done with it, so let's add --rm here. Next, we need the image name. There are lots of images that are configure with QEMU user static in them. The one I like to use is aptman/qus. This is from the Docker Hub, and so we can use it here easily. Finally, we'll need to provide some arguments to qus. Specifically, the arguments we need are --static, then two dashes, then --path, and the name of the instructions set
>
> **[7:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=468)** we want QEMU to emulate, or arm, in this case. So once again, that's docker run --privilege, the really dangerous one, --rm aptman/qus --static, then two dashes, then --path arm, let's run it. After we ran this, we got a message from qus stating that the binary handler has been registered, which means that we're good to go, and you'll see that it gets registered for a lot of different processor types. All these are within the ARM family, hence, why you can see a ton of them here. So now that we've enabled the binary format handlers, and next thing we need to do is install QEMU into our VM. Without us doing this, when we try to start containers in the other instruction set, they'll fail with a really weird message about not being able to find anything. When in the background, it's really not being able to find QEMU. It'll be really confusing, so it's probably better to get it done now. So since I'm on a Mac, running Docker within a Lima virtual machine, I'm going to type limactl shell docker. This will put us into our VM, and then the next thing I'm going to do is I'm going to run sudo apt -y update to update the repository programs that I can install from aptitude. This might take a minute, depending on the speed of your internet connection, and then we're going to go ahead and install QEMU dependency. So to do that, I'm going to type
>
> **[9:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=561)** pseudo app -y install qemu qemu-system-misc qemu-user-static qemu-user binfmt support. Now, depending on the recency of your version of [[Ubuntu]], or the operating system that you're using you might not actually need to do this. It might already have these programs, but in case you don't, this is how you would install them. You might get this scary message saying something about kernel and compatibilities. You just hit okay, hit tab and then okay, again. Okay, now that we've installed it, let's hit exit, Exit, and that will bring us back into our terminal session. From here, let's run a quick smoke test make sure that this is working. To do that, I am going to type docker run --rm --platform linux/arm, and then I'll put Alpine and then uname. So what this is going to do is start a container, specifically a container from the Alpine Docker image, and just run the program uname like we saw earlier, but it's going to run it with the ARM instruction set instead of the Intel X86 instruction set. And there we go. If we get Linux back, then we know that we are in business. You can type uname -a, and then confirm that it has arm at the end. If you don't get Linux, and you get something like "no such file or directory",
>
> **[10:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=654)** then that means that QEMU probably failed to install. So just keep that in mind. Okay, so now that we've installed the QEMU binfmt handlers as well as QEMU, we should be able to now build our ARM compatible image. But first we need to build the X86 image. So to do that, we're going to go back to our Docker build command, so we're going to type docker build, and we're going to create two images. We're going to create one for X86 and another one for arm. So an easy way to denote the two is instead of just having "my-image ." here, we'll put a colon after my image, and then put latest-x86. Now, another parameter that I'm going to provide here is the --pull parameter. And the reason why I'm going to provide this is because, this ensures that we're downloading the base image for the correct architecture that we want to build against. So what this is going to do is it's going to pull the X86 version of Ubuntu instead of the ARM version, which might happen if we were building the ARM version first. So let's go ahead and run this, and there we go. Nothing exciting there, so now let's try building the ARM version. So to do that I'm going to replace X86 with arm, and then I'm going to add another parameter called --platform. When we use this with Docker run, it's at the architecture of the base image that we wanted to use there. Here it's going to set the architecture of the base image that we download when we process the Docker file.
>
> **[12:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=751)** So instead of pulling the X86 version of Ubuntu, it's going to pull the ARM version. So let's go ahead and specify Linux ARM so that we do that, and then we'll leave the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] as it is. So let's go ahead and enter and see what happens. We'll see some warning messages here saying that the platform that we've requested doesn't match the platform on our host. That's okay because that's the magic of binfmt or bin format. So let's look at this run command here. Normally, if we try to run this without the --platform argument, if we were downloading an ARMbase base image, this wouldn't work. We would get the exec format error that we saw before, but because bin format configured our system to send arm-based commands to QEMU so that they can be emulated, this run statement went to QEMU, QEMU translated it into X86 compatible instructions, and it's executing them here. Now, one of the disadvantages of doing this is that that translation is slower than just native code. So you might have noticed that all of these get lines that are here were a little bit slower than what we've seen before, and that's the reason why this translation from ARM to X86 is a little bit costly. And as we can see, it's still running because of those translations working in the background. So that's just something to keep in mind. If you're going to be building cross platform images like this, you can expect a delay in platforms that are not native to your machine.
>
> **[14:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-platform-images?u=76281980&t=846)** Okay, we finally finished. So let's go ahead and give the ARM version of shot. So to do that, I'm going to run docker run --rm my-image:latest-arm, and there we go, works just like I did before. But interestingly, take a look at this. Look at the command that we're running. As you can see here, the bin format command is actually what's running our entry point. This is happening transparently. I didn't need to make any changes to the Docker file for it to do this. That's the advantage of installing qus, and configuring binfmt with it, this automatically happens. It happens even before a Docker gets involved. This is like an automatic Linux thing, which is really, really cool. We're almost done here, we have the two images. We have one for X86 and one for ARM. The final thing for us to do is to create the manifest list that puts 'em together. Again, because we need a registry for this, we're going to learn how to do that in Chapter Nine, so stay tuned.

#### [Multi-app images](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-app-images?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-app-images?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about the final trick we can do with dockerfiles, running multiple applications within the same container. By design, containers are meant to run a single application at a time. This is part of what makes containers as portable and flexible as they are. However, there are times when running a single application isn't enough. Some applications, particularly older ones, might have many components that need to coexist on the same system. They might share memory or other resources that are difficult to spread out amongst multiple containers. While many of these applications are being rewritten to be more modular, plenty of them still exist. Another useful scenario for a multi app or a multi-process container is for emulating a virtual machine without actually creating one. I've done this in the past while testing [[Ansible]] playbooks as an example. Instead of creating [[Virtual Machines]] that take up tons of space and waiting on them to start, I simply created containers that had just enough in them to emulate a VM, pointing Ansible at those, and tested the results. One other useful scenario I've seen for multi app containers is emulating a cluster of services in a single container for simplicity. For example, the [[Kubernetes]] in Docker project still does this today. Kubernetes in Docker makes it easy to run an entire Kubernetes cluster within Docker. While there are five components that make up a working Kubernetes cluster, kind creates a single container that runs all of them simultaneously. While this isn't ideal for production workloads it works really well for locally testing your app without a ton of overhead. Despite these scenarios, you usually don't want to do this.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-app-images?u=76281980&t=98)** Containers are designed to be started and stopped at will. Ideally, your applications should keep running or recover quickly when this happens. Containers with multiple applications within them usually have more complicated start-up procedures than containers with a single application. This makes recovery more difficult which can lead to extended downtime. Additionally, it could be more difficult to know where stuff is throughout your platform if containers are running multiple things at once. Let's say that we're running docker ps on a system we're troubleshooting. We see 20 containers on there with random names but they all start with the name of our app. It would be really difficult to know that, say, five of them are also running [[Databases]], message queues, and random app servers within each of them, unless you exec'ed into each one and looked at their process tables. At that point, you might as well run virtual machines that will stick around longer and likely have monitoring agents on them that can tell you this information. Finally, multi-app containers usually take longer to start than single app containers. This can make elasticity difficult, and if you're in a cloud potentially more expensive. Remember, you usually pay per second or per minute on cloud container platforms. There are a few alternatives to running multiple apps in a single container. The first popular alternative is to break up your application's dependencies into multiple containers that exist on the same container network. In our example, the database, message queues, and random app servers would be their own containers but they would all exist in a single network. This way, they can communicate with each other
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-app-images?u=76281980&t=192)** over the network instead of relying on shared memory. We'll learn more about container networking in a later chapter. Creating this setup with Docker commands alone can be challenging. Docker [[Jetpack Compose|Compose]] is a tool provided by Docker that simplifies this. Compose allows you to express all of your application's dependencies as individual services in a manifest file. Once you create the manifest, Compose handles the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]]. While this course doesn't cover Compose there are plenty of great courses on [[LinkedIn]] Learning that dive deep into it. The next level beyond Compose is using a container orchestrator that can create, move, and coordinate containers throughout multiple machines. This can help make your app extremely scalable and work reliably regardless of where services live. Kubernetes is an example of a popular container orchestrator used by companies of all sizes all over the world. Check out my Kubernetes, Your First Project course if you want to dive right into creating your own clusters. Regardless, because this is Docker Essential Training there are probably scenarios you'll run up against in your container operations journey. Learning how to do this will be useful, so let's talk about the three popular ways multi app container images are built today. The easiest way to create a multi app container image is through entrypoint scripts. We learned earlier in this chapter that we can configure the entrypoint command in our dockerfiles to launch an entrypoint script that handles launching our app. We can extend on this concept by configuring our entrypoint script to run multiple apps at once.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-app-images?u=76281980&t=284)** Here we have an entrypoint script that invokes two processes, my first process and my second process. To launch both of them in the background, we simply put an ampersand at the end of their lines. We then wait for any of these processes to exit before finally exiting the script and ending our container. When we create a container for this image with docker run it will make bash the container's parent process or pid1. As we learned in our sections on cmd and entrypoint, this has implications around signal passing. Let's say we hit Control+C to terminate this container but my second process is a really slow process. While the bash process that started our entrypoint script will exit, there's no guarantee that my second process will receive the signal as well. If it doesn't, my second process will continue to run in the background despite its parent process being killed. This can become problematic. To prevent this from happening, we would need to add --init to our docker run command. This configures our container to run our entrypoint script within a process supervisor called tini. Tini's job will be to watch every process running in your container and making sure that signals are passed correctly to them. This way, when we Control+C our container, tini makes sure that everything the container started gets shut down with it. While this approach works fine in practice there are simple ways of managing multiple services running within a system. It would be nice if, for example, we could have a folder defining all of the services we want to start with some configuration around how to start them. So like systemd, you might be thinking?
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-app-images?u=76281980&t=381)** Exactly. It's totally possible to run systemd inside of a container. However, I personally will only do it if you absolutely have to. systemd was designed to be run on a system that never shuts down. It also does a lot of other stuff like manage control groups, resolving DNS records, and managing network interfaces or basically everything that docker uses in some way or another in the background. This usually requires a lot of extra volume mounts to directories that I wouldn't recommend doing in a plain container. Creating a Docker image that runs stuff with systemd can be a bit challenging. These instructions shown here demonstrate how to do this. However, if you're working with applications that start systemd units and converting into anything else would be a challenge, it's definitely doable. Fortunately, there are options between the super simple tini supervisor and the systemd juggernaut. s6-overlay is one of these. s6 does the same job as tini, IE watching processes and passing signals to them, but it also allows users to express multiple service in a convenient directory structure. It is also designed for use in containers, which means it can handle being rudely terminated really nicely compared to heavier alternatives like systemd or supervisord. This image over here provides an example of a dockerfile configured with s6-overlay. Created by [[GitHub]] user madewithlove, this dockerfile creates an image that runs both [[PHP]] and NGINX. This is really useful for [[Web Servers]] as it allows it to execute server side PHP scripts which is very common.
>
> **[7:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/multi-app-images?u=76281980&t=476)** While I didn't provide an example of this in practice, there's lots of great literature on this topic if you're interested in learning more. Check out the links here to get started. Archived versions are also provided in the exercise files for this section.

This means that anyone who's pulling this from the Docker hub, for example, will only need to download a two megabyte file instead of downloading this monstrosity over here. This also means that because this image only contains our test app, the likelihood of it having security vulnerabilities is much, much smaller than this app that contains a whole bunch of other stuff. I hope that was fun. I hope you enjoyed it.

If the thought of containers running this root by default has scared you in the past, this section might help reduce some of those concerns. Let's go back all the way to chapter one for a minute. In container run times, we learned that we can further restrict what apps can do within those namespaces by adding or removing Linux capabilities from it. Since containers are just a fusion of namespaces and control groups, Docker makes it really easy to do this right from the command line. Linux capabilities are groups of privileges that could be bestowed onto applications, like creating new files or sending network packets. This allows admins to do things like give applications the ability to do everything but change file ownership or give applications to do nothing but set and get the time. Since applications within containers usually run as root by default, being able to have control over what they can do goes a long way towards preventing them from unexpectedly harming their own hosts. #### [Setting container limits: Part 1](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/setting-container-limits-part-1?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/setting-container-limits-part-1?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we know a little more about hardening our containers, let's go one step further and learn about setting container resource limits. Being able to control resource consumption is really important. Most machines running Docker will have many handfuls of containers running within them. If one of those containers is running, say, a faulty application, that is quickly leaking memory, this container can not only starve other containers from memory they need to run their applications, but can also potentially bring the entire system to a grinding halt. In the best case, this is just really annoying. At worst, this memory starvation can incur significant downtime and damage to your business. Fortunately, Docker makes it really easy to place limits on your containers. We learned in container run times that containers are composed of name spaces and control groups. Name spaces specify the resources that containers can see or access on a system. Whereas control groups specify how much of those resources containers can consume. Control groups allow you to define rules like, this app can only use two CPUs, or this app can use no more than 128 megabytes of memory. Docker Run has several options for setting limits. The most popular ones are dash, dash CPUs, and dash dash memory. As the option name suggest, these flags allow you to define the maximum processor time and memory and bytes a container can have, respectively. They use the CPU and memory control groups on the host system to enforce these limits. What do I mean by maximum processor time?
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/setting-container-limits-part-1?u=76281980&t=96)** This concept requires some explanation. You'll notice here that the dash dash CPUs flag accepts a decimal. This is because the CPU control group allows you to specify the maximum amount of processor time a process within it can consume. It does not mean use this many CPU course, as the option might suggest. In fact, dash dash CPU is a permutation of two other flags. Dash dash CPU dash period, and dash dash CPU dash quota. Dash dash CPU dash period defines a period of time the operating system kernel should wait before giving your container processing time in microseconds. CPU quota defines the amount of processing time your container should have across all the CPUs on your system, once a processor gives your container time as per CPU period. Also on microseconds. This concept is best explained with a few examples. Let's say that your system has a single processor and it's running some applications on it, let's say four or five. Behind the scenes, the container is running a program called a scheduler that is responsible for scheduling processor time amongst these applications. The scheduler is going to do its best to make sure that all 10 applications get a fair amount of processing time. Let's say that you want to ensure that your application gets at least 40% of the processor's time for as long as it's running. The CPU control group allows us to specify the amount of time the schedule should wait
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/setting-container-limits-part-1?u=76281980&t=189)** before giving your application processor time and the amount of processing time it should get, once that time arrives. Since we want at least 40% of the processor at any given time, we can translate that into; ensure that I get processor time every second, and make sure to give me 400 milliseconds every time that happens. Put another way, we can set the CPU period to one million microseconds because remember, it takes microseconds. And CPU quota to 400,000 microseconds. Now, let's say that we've upgraded the system to two CPUs. We've now effectively doubled the number of tasks that the system can process at one time. What does that mean for our time-restricted application, though? We said that we want the scheduler to allocate the processor for us every second, for 40 milliseconds at a time. But remember, quotas and periods apply for every processor in your system. Because a scheduler can now run two things at once, while we're still getting 40% of a single processor's time, we're getting 20% of the total amount of time that's available. This isn't what we want, though. We want the entire 40%. This means that we need to either tell the scheduler to visit us more often, or increase the amount of time we take up every time we get scheduled. Translating that to CPU quota and CPU period, we can change this to be either a CPU quota of 40,000 and a CPU period of 500,000 milliseconds. The CPU option simplifies this work
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/setting-container-limits-part-1?u=76281980&t=282)** by giving Docker a fractional amount of CPU time we want, in decimal. In this scenario, setting CPUs to 1.4 would be the same as setting the CPU quota and CPU combinations we put together above. Technically, since Docker sets a default CPU period of 100 milliseconds, this is the same as setting CPU period to 100,000, and CPU quota to 140,000 in our two processor example system. Okay, that's enough theory. Let's actually see this in practice.

Without getting super deep into the details what stress is doing here is it's telling the kernel, Hey, I want 256 megs of memory every few seconds until that vm bytes limit is reached. And because the kernel is nice,
>
> **[8:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/setting-container-limits-part-2?u=76281980&t=533)** the kernel will keep giving it those 256 megabyte chunks as needed. However, the memory control group to which this container is attached has a hard limit of two gigabytes. But that never really happens and so the container just hangs there. So I wouldn't recommend actually using this flag. Instead, I would just rely on the kernel terminating the application as it should when the memory limit is reached.

#### [Configuring logging with logging drivers: Part 1](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-logging-with-logging-drivers-part-1?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-logging-with-logging-drivers-part-1?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] We've seen how easy it is to retrieve logs from containers with the Docker [[CLI]]. However, we've never talked about how Docker actually retrieves those logs. As it happens, there are lots of ways to configure how containers log stuff. Let's learn more about them. Most applications will eventually need to print things to a console while they're running. [[Linux]] gives them two channels to send those messages through: standard out, or stdout, and standard error, or stderr. E-R-R. There's also stdin, or standard in, which allows applications to receive input, but we'll put that aside for now. Since these channels are designed to always receive data, they are more commonly known as streams. The standard out stream is used for regular informational messages that users usually don't need to do anything with. The standard error stream, as the name suggests, is used for error messages that should be actioned upon. This isn't always the case, however. Some applications, like cURL, for example, will send debug logs and other access information into standard error. When an application is running inside of a container, Docker uses logging drivers to capture data sent to these streams. Logging drivers are programs that forward data sent to standard out or standard error streams to somewhere else, like a folder on your disk or an online log-aggregation platform. This can be really useful for understanding how your applications behave or analyzing data omitted by your applications. There are two ways through which logging drivers can be configured. The first way is through the --log-driver
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-logging-with-logging-drivers-part-1?u=76281980&t=93)** and --log-opts flags and the docker run or docker container create commands.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-logging-with-logging-drivers-part-1?u=76281980&t=99)** - -log-driver allows you to specify the logging driver to use, where as --log-ops is used to provide options to it.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-logging-with-logging-drivers-part-1?u=76281980&t=108)** - -log-opts accepts a [[JSON]] object. These properties will vary between drivers. By default, retrieving logs from containers is a blocking operation. This just means that your container and any applications within it will pause while the logging driver retrieves your logs. Because the default logging driver stores logs on disk, this usually won't slow things down. However, some logging drivers perform extra work between the time your application fires off a message to a standard out or standard error stream and the time it marks your log as processed. This can add undesirable slowness to your application. Fortunately, you can add the logging option mode=nonblocking, which you can see here on your screen, to docker run or docker container create to tell Docker to not block your container when this happens. While this will minimize disruptions to your application from logging drivers, this might cause data loss if containers exceed the space they've been given for log storage. Okay, now that we know how logging drivers work, let's talk about the drivers there are available. Docker comes with many other logging drivers. Additional logging drivers can be installed as Docker plugins. Given the vast differences between each driver, I won't cover all of them here. Docker has great documentation and configuration instructions for each driver. You can click this link to check that out. One driver that I will cover is the json-file driver. Docker uses this driver by default. JSON files store messages emitted to logging streams in JSON format. This might be surprising,
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-logging-with-logging-drivers-part-1?u=76281980&t=200)** given that output from Docker logs doesn't look like JSON. However, this is one of the benefits of the JSON file logging format. Each message is stored as an object. This object contains numerous properties about the message, like the time it was created, the container that created it, and, of course, the message itself. This makes searching for specific messages based on granular data much easier to do, and we'll see an example of that soon.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[JSON]] (6), [[CLI]] (1), [[Linux]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (13), curl (1)
> **Env Vars:** json (5), cli (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), is a  (1), means that (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** curl (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Prerequisites:** configure (1)

>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/configuring-the-docker-daemon?u=76281980&t=227)** - -Add-runtime allows you to tell the Docker engine about a different OCI compliant runtime to use. You can provide options to the runtime with --exact-opt. They can look something like properties.doTheThing=false. Notice that it's a key value option just like add runtime is. Now, these options will differ from runtime to runtime, so you will need to consult the run times documentation for those details. Finally, --default-runtime tells the Docker engine which runtime it should use by default. It uses runc by default here, but if you wanted to use something like crun which we learned about earlier, you would enable that here. Here are all the useful Docker options that we just covered.
