#### [The case for Docker in Docker](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-case-for-docker-in-docker?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-case-for-docker-in-docker?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We have finally arrived at the Docker in Docker chapter. You might be wondering, why are we covering this in an essentials course? That is a fantastic question, so let's talk about it. We already know how easy Docker makes running applications consistently across platforms and environments. However, what if your app actually needs to interact with the Docker engine itself? There are several reasons why you might find yourself in this situation. Let's say that you're writing tests for your app, but want your test suite to behave the same way on any machine like [[GitHub]] Actions Runners. Since the operating system and [[Hardware]] of the build servers running your test will not be consistent, having your test run as containers that then start your app also as a container is useful. Or let's say that you need to write an application that needs to create or manage containers. This often comes up in platform engineering, developer environments, SSH junk boxes, and single use containers for running jobs are examples of situations where an application like this can be useful. Speaking of platform engineering, how about situations where you want to emulate entire platforms on your machine to better replicate your production environment? For example, your job processor might run as its own container but it might want to create additional containers to handle load from your app. This is where learning how to run Docker within Docker can come in handy. Docker and Docker is exactly what it looks like. Managing containers created by Docker within a docker container.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/the-case-for-docker-in-docker?u=76281980&t=94)** Those of you who've managed [[Virtual Machines]] in the past might be familiar with running VMs within VMs. Docker and Docker is very similar to that concept. In this chapter, I'm going to teach you how to install and test Docker within Docker, and we're going to test that everything is working correctly, and there's some gotchas that you need to be aware of, so we'll talk about those as well. Let's go.

#### [Creating the container to run containers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-the-container-to-run-containers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-the-container-to-run-containers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's start by creating a container that will create other containers. There are two ways to do this in the wild. The first way involves installing the Docker engine within the container like we did back when we learned how to install Docker the hard way. Some programs like [[Kubernetes]] and Docker do this to be able to truly create containers within containers. The second way is to mount Docker's UNIX socket on your container, usually located at /var/lib/docker.sock to a file within a container and install just a Docker client instead of a full-blown engine. The first approach was more common when Docker was new. However, there are numerous issues with this technique. First, Docker relies on version two of the cgroup driver. This driver is used to mount control groups to containers that it creates. While Docker handles mounting control groups within the container transparently, trying to use those control groups within Docker is possible, but not straightforward. Secondly, Docker container store data in an Overlay file system, which we learned about earlier. We learned then that Overlay file systems work by presenting a folder containing a unified view of other folders and using copy ups and white outs to add and remove files to and from the file system. Consequently, Overlay file systems cannot overlay Overlay file systems. Say that 10 times fast. Since container file systems use overlays by default, this prevents Docker from working. There are workarounds around this, but they're also not straightforward.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-the-container-to-run-containers?u=76281980&t=93)** However, even if you work around all these problems, the child containers that get created within the parent container won't be accessible on your machine. This can complicate things. The second way and the one we're going to be using is much simpler. Instead of attempting to run the Docker engine within Docker, we're going to configure our container to communicate with our existing Docker engine and create containers from it. We'll do this by bind mounting the UNIX socket used by the Docker engine to the container. Since this will create containers directly from wherever the Docker engine is running, this almost eliminates all the hassles above. There are some gotchas to be aware of, but they're manageable. We're going to learn how to do Docker quote unquote in Docker through the second approach. You'll see the strategy used more often as you learn more about containers and it is generally safer and easier to use. However, if you're really interested in doing quote unquote pure Docker in Docker, I recommend taking a look at Sysbox. This is the Sysbox project on [[GitHub]]. Sysbox is a container runtime that makes it really easy to run Docker within Docker. It does this through a special runtime that handles all of the nitty gritty details of creating and managing containers within a container. Sysbox is open source, as you can see. You can view it on GitHub and check out its documentation here. All right, let's open up a terminal and get started. First, we're going to create the container that will create other containers. To do that, I'm going to type
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-the-container-to-run-containers?u=76281980&t=184)** docker run --rm -v /var/run/docker.sock
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-the-container-to-run-containers?u=76281980&t=193)** mapping it to /var/run/docker.sock --entrypoint bash -i -t
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-the-container-to-run-containers?u=76281980&t=203)** and then we can use the my-image image that we created earlier. Cool, we're in the container and I realize that this concept is new. This concept is called bind mounting and we'll learn about it later in this course. Okay, so since our image includes curl, let's go ahead and use that to make sure that we can communicate with the Docker engine. So first, I'm going to type curl. Next, we need to tell it that we're communicating with the UNIX socket which we can do by providing --unix.socket. Then we need to put the path to the UNIX socket which is going to be /var/run/docker/sock. Finally, we'll provide the URL. Since we're going to be talking to the Docker engine through HTTP, we'll start by typing http:// here. Next, we need to provide a host name. Since we're talking through a UNIX socket, we actually don't need a valid host name here. We can put anything we want, like anything for example. Finally, we need to provide an endpoint within the Docker engine that we want to interact with. The Docker engine has a lot of endpoints. Check out the link here to read them all. Since we're just testing that we can communicate with the Docker engine, let's try to list containers. The endpoint to do that is /containers/[[JSON]]. Let's see what happens here. Success. We get some stuff back. We now know that the Docker engine is reachable and we're now ready to install a real Docker client. Also, here's a fun fact about what you're looking at. The objects in this JSON array are the exact same things
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-the-container-to-run-containers?u=76281980&t=296)** you would see if you ran Docker inspect which since we don't have Docker installed yet, we can't do that here, but we'll take care of that next.

#### [Limitations of Docker through Docker](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/limitations-of-docker-through-docker?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/limitations-of-docker-through-docker?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we learned a little about running Docker in Docker through sharing the Docker engine Unix socket, let's demonstrate some limitations that come with this approach. One disadvantage of this approach is that we can see other containers, volumes, networks and other resources managed by the Docker engine we're sharing. We can also use those resources from within this container. To verify that, if I do docker ps, like we did earlier, we could see the portainer container running. If this portainer container was for something secret, this would be a really bad thing. The only way to work around this is to go pure Docker in Docker with something like Sysbox. The reason for that is that approach keeps everything isolated within its own Docker engine while still being managed by the Docker engine on your virtual machine. However, the much bigger limitation that you'll run into more often is with mounted volumes. Let me demonstrate. We have two terminals here. As usual, the terminal on the left is for our VM and the terminal on the right is our actual machine. So let me actually log into my VM with, limactl shell docker. So since this container that creates other containers was created from the my-image container image that we created earlier, we know that we have the /app directory in it. So let's say we wanted to make /app/include/header.txt available to some other container that we want to create.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/limitations-of-docker-through-docker?u=76281980&t=96)** One way we can do that is with a bind mount. And to do that with the bind mount, we would do, docker run --rm -it, and then we would do -v, the file that we want to mount is /app/include/header.txt and the file we want to present this as within the container, we can just make it /header.txt. And then finally, we'll put alpine. Now, I'm putting /it here to denote that I want to interact with this container, just like we're doing with our parent container. Now that we're in, let's say I want to read the contents of that file by running cat /header.txt. This is really weird. It's saying that /header.txt is a directory. That really doesn't make sense to me. So I'm going to use stats /header.txt to see if cat is just lying to me and it's not, because if I look at this access property, the first thing I see is a d, which tells me that this is, indeed, a directory. And then it also tells me here that it's a directory. So that's really strange. So I'm going to take a look at the file inside of this container to see whether that's a directory. So I'll use stat, as well. And that's weird, it says it's a regular file here. So what gives, why is it that when I run this, it takes this file and mounts it as a directory? That doesn't make any sense.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/limitations-of-docker-through-docker?u=76281980&t=191)** Well, if you recall from our chapter on storage, which we covered earlier, Docker assumes that any file that is bind mounted that doesn't exist on the host gets created as a directory on the host because we're sharing the Docker engine's Unix socket with this container, it's using the context of the machine that's running the Docker engine, not the context of our container. So what we really did when we ran this command, was tell the Docker engine in our virtual machine, hey, go ahead and bind mount the /app/include/header.txt file on your actual machine to /header.txt file in this container, please. Docker engine has no idea that this command came from within a container and it just gets it done. Okay, so this directory got created on the host. Where is it, you might be asking. That's a great question. Could it possibly be in our VM at, say, /app/include/header.txt? Could it actually be here? Well, that's weird. I didn't get anything. So that signifies that it probably is. So what happens if I stat this? That's weird. Look at that. It's actually a directory in our virtual machine, which is not what we expected and that's definitely not what we wanted, right? We were expecting to share this file within our container as a file within our child container. That's what we were expecting. So there really isn't a way around this except to use Docker volumes.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/limitations-of-docker-through-docker?u=76281980&t=286)** Let me show you what I mean here. So let me clear out the screen. I'm going to create a new volume called temp. Now, remember that this volume will not exist in this container, it will live inside of the VM. This won't be a problem for us though, because we're not going to copy our app directory into this volume. Since we can only copy into Docker volumes through containers, like we saw in the storage chapter, we'll just go ahead and create our child container now without attaching to it. We can do that with docker container create, instead of using docker run. Here, I'm going to skip --rm, since we're going to remove it manually. Next, I'll put in -v, but instead of putting /app/include/header.txt here, I'm going to put the name of my volume, or temp, followed by a colon. And then next, I'll put the directory I want to bind this volume to, let's call it /tmp. Finally, let's put the name of an image. We can use any image here, it doesn't matter. Let's call it alpine. And let's hit enter. So we got a container ID back, which means that this has been created. We're going to take note of the ID here, specifically the first four, and we're just going to copy that. I could have also given this container a name, like copier or something, but I'm just going to stick to the ID. Next, I'm going to use docker cp to copy our /app directory into the volume, which we can do through our 820A container, like this. And I'm going to copy it into /app within the container as well, or within the volume as well.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/limitations-of-docker-through-docker?u=76281980&t=380)** Now, remember, the volume is mounted at /tmp, so I have to put /tmp here and then put /app. Great. So that has been copied. Note that we have debug mode on since we turned it on quite a while ago. If you didn't have debug mode on, you would see nothing here. Finally, let's go ahead and start that child container with docker run, but this time, we're going to change our -v statement here
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/limitations-of-docker-through-docker?u=76281980&t=411)** so that instead of mounting the header.txt files to each other we're going to go ahead and mount the temp volume to this container at, say, tmp. Now that we're in, let's go ahead and try and cat that header file again. So I'm going to do cat /tmp/app/include/header.txt. And there it is. This is exactly what we wanted.

### 7. Networking

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Introduction to container networking](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-container-networking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-container-networking?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Docker has a very strong networking model. Connecting containers with each other into the outside world is very easy to do with Docker. You can even customize the underlying network technology used by your containers. However, networking within containers works a little differently than you might be used to. This is a simplified diagram of a typical networking session on your computer. The content on this page was sent from a server somewhere in the [[LinkedIn]] Learning data center, to a gateway router, possibly also within their data center. These packets hopped from router to router until they made it to your router at home. Upon arrival, your router sent these packets to a [[NIC|network interface card]], or a [[NIC]], on your machine. Your operating system picked up these packets, looked at them, noticed, hey, this is [[HTML]], and some other web stuff, and sent them right along to your browser, with a few extra steps along the way, of course. This slide shows the same transaction but from the perspective of a container. Everything about how the packets containing this webpage's content is the same until it's time for your router to send off the packets to your machine. Your operating system will open these packets, see that they're destined for containers, and pass the packets off to Docker to have it deal with them. Almost every container engine's networking model is based on plug-ins. These plug-ins describe how these packets get routed to containers. Some plug-ins will send these packets to containers through a virtual networking card on your machine. Other plugins might give containers quote unquote real network cards that work with the network card on your machine
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-container-networking?u=76281980&t=94)** to receive packets appropriately. There is even one plugin that simply discards these packets. As we can see here, container networking through container engines, like the Docker engine is very powerful. In this chapter, we're going to learn all about how networking works within containers and the Docker engine. We'll explore popular networking plugins and create containers with them to learn how they work. We'll also learn about overlay networking, an interesting networking concept that is used heavily by container orchestrators like [[Kubernetes]], Docker Swarm, and Nomad. Finally, we'll learn how to expose networked services running within containers to your machine. This will allow you to run [[Web Servers]], [[Databases]], or basically any networked application you can think of within Docker as if you are running them on your own machine. Let's get started.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Creating Docker networks with bridge-mode networking](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-networks-with-bridge-mode-networking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-networks-with-bridge-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's very briefly go back to the simple app that we've been using up until now. We know that this app will attempt to visit [google.com](https://google.com) to get today's date unless a file is defined. However, we never really thought about how the container does this. How did our operating system know to send [google.com](https://google.com) package to docker? How did this network driver thing know how to send those packets to our container? The answer is pretty simple. Every container uses the bridge docker network by default. The bridge network driver connects docker containers to a virtual bridge network installed on your machine. With the little [[Linux]] and IP tables magic, containers connected to this bridge network will automatically be used to communicate with other containers connected to the bridge as well as to the outside world. Let's learn more about how this happens. A bridge network is a virtual network device that connects multiple networks together into a single network. Bridge networks are useful for taking traffic from a real network adapter and forwarding it to virtual network adapters, like those on VMs or containers as we'll soon learn. We learned a while back that every container will quote unquote "unshare" one or more Linux name spaces. One of those name spaces is the net namespace. This namespace allows containers within it to get their very own network stack that's isolated from the network stack on your host, including network adapters. Connecting these virtual nets into a common bridge allows containers to talk to each other while remaining logically isolated with some exceptions. Additionally, docker uses IP tables rules to ensure
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-networks-with-bridge-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=97)** that bridges are isolated from each other. There are a few other properties about the bridge driver. Like I said earlier, bridge is the default network driver used by docker containers. Creatively, docker also creates a default bridge network called, Bridge. No configuration is needed to have containers join the Bridge bridge network. You can also create bridge networks of your own. Containers can be joined to these networks with the --net option. They can even be joined to multiple bridge networks at a time. We'll see this in action in a few minutes. While containers connected to the default bridge network can talk to each other, they have to know each other's IP addresses. While the docker engine does have an embedded DNS server, it would be really disconcerting to be able to connect to any container on the bridge by name, regardless of whether you started it or not. Custom bridge networks do not have this limitation. You can provide the --link option to docker run or modify each container /Etsy/host files to override this. However, if DNS is important to you, I recommend using a custom bridge network. All right, let's drop into a terminal to take a closer look at docker networks. Docker has an entire sub command for working with networks. It's called docker network, and it looks like this. There are a number of things we can do here, as you can see. So let's start with the docker network LS command, which you can see here. As you can see, we have three networks, bridge, host, and none.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-networks-with-bridge-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=190)** We'll learn about host and none later in this chapter. In the meantime, let's use docker network inspect bridge to learn a little more about how this network is configured. So I'm going to clear my screen and run docker network inspect bridge. There's a lot going on here so let's take a closer look at some of these properties. Every network has a name and an ID. These are defined by the name and ID properties shown here. While this network is called Bridge, we can see that the driver that's powering this network is also called Bridge. We can also see some IP address configuration data in this config block here. Every bridge network will have a gateway IP address and a subnet expressed in the classless inter domain [[Routing]] notation or CIDR or C-I-D-R. In other words, this just tells us where packets from containers will get routed to and how big the network is respectively. In this case, the bridge has a subnet of 172.17.0.0/16 and it's gateway is going to be 172.17.0.1. This means that any container joining this bridge will get an IP address starting with 172.17 and all network packets from these containers will flow through 172.17.0.1 first. The /16 here indicates how big this network is. In other words, the /16 indicates how many IP addresses will fit into this network.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-networks-with-bridge-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=284)** An easy formula to use to determine the number of IP addresses that will fit within a network is to take the number after the slash in this case 16, and then use this formula, two to the power of whatever that number is, 16 in this case, minus two. So notice how the two to the power of 16 is grouped between these parentheses. You take the result of this and subtract two from it. You do not do two to the power 16 minus two. The two is outside of the group. So in this case, 65,534 AP addresses can fit in this network which means about 65,000 nodes can fit within this network. Now, it's very unlikely you're going to have a docker network that big, but it's good to know how to do these calculations just in case. Now, if you don't want to do this all in your head, it's very easy to do this online. The page that I like to use is [calculator.net](https://calculator.net) search for subnet. And there you go. Just click on the IP subnet calculator and then inside of the IP address, go ahead and put in the IP address of the gateway, in this case .1 and then select the size of your network. In this case, it's going to be /16. And then go ahead and calculate that. And just like we saw earlier, we get the total number of hosts and the total number of usable hosts. And this also gives you the IP addresses that fit within this network, which is really handy.

#### [Exposing container ports between containers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Docker makes it easy to expose network ports within containers to other containers, or even to your local machine. Let me show you what I mean. We have our two terminals from last time, each running a container. The top terminal is running container-a and the bottom is running container-b. In container-a, I'm going to use a program called [[Netcat]], or nc, to start a really simple TCP server on port 80. The server will do nothing but echo whatever text it receives. To do that, I'll first run ifconfig eth0 to show our container's IP address, which is here. Then I'll run nc -l -p 80 to start the server. We won't see anything after we run this because it's waiting for someone or something to send input to it. In container-b, I'll connect to container-a's server with Telnet. Telnet is a program that allows you to talk to servers behind TCP ports as if they were terminals. We'll need to install it first. To do that, I'll type apk add busybox-extras. Now, I won't be able to do this because I'm not logged in as root, and unfortunately this container doesn't have sudo, so what we'll need to do is we'll need to restart container-b as the root user. So to do that I'm going to exit. Then I'm going to stop container-b. Then I'm going to remove container-b
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=93)** because we're going to need to reconfigure it. Then we're going to recreate container-b, so let me go back to my create command from earlier. And then, before the -it, I'm going to go ahead and put
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=107)** - -user is root. So we get a container ID back, so from here, I'm going to start container-b again, and then attach to it. And now, as you can see, we have a root shell because we have a pound sign instead of a dollar sign. So let's go ahead and continue adding busybox-extras. Once that's done, we can confirm that Telnet is installed by running telnet --help. Since we see a help page, we know that the installation succeeded. Okay, let's try to connect to TCP port 80 on container-a via Telnet. To do that, I'm going to run telnet 172.18.0.2 80. Let's clear our screen and run this. So as we expected, this is hanging. It's hanging because these containers are in separate networks. Eventually, container-b will time out trying to connect to container-a. We learned how to join container-a to network B in the last video to work around this, but let's say that we only wanted to make this one port available to container-b, or any other container that wishes to connect to it. To do that, we can quote unquote publish container-a's port 80 by mapping it to a port on our own machine. Port publishing uses iptables, a rules engine for filtering [[Linux]] packets to quote unquote rewrite packets destined for a container's IP address and to a port on your machine. By doing this, we can connect to the container through
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=201)** the container's bridge network gateway on our own host. If you've ever configured port forwarding on your router to connect to something from the internet, then this is very similar. So let's learn how to do that. I'm going to stop the server on container-a by pressing Ctrl+C. Then press Ctrl+D to exit the terminal. Next, we'll need to remove container-a since publishing ports requires a container to be recreated. So just like we did for container-b, I'm going to do container rm container-a. Next, I'm going to go back to the docker container create command for a, which we can see here. But before running this, after the network-a option, at the end, I'm going to go ahead and put --publish, and then I'm going to put 8080:80. So, what are these numbers? Well, publish takes two numbers separated by a colon: the port on your host to access the container from on the left, and the port within the container that is being published on the right. So in this example, we're mapping port 8080 on our host to port 80 within the container. An easy way for me to remember this is OUTSIDE:INSIDE. The port outside of the container goes on the left and the port inside of the container goes on the right. Why did I choose 8080 here instead of just 80? I chose 8080 here because port 80 is an example of a privileged or quote unquote low-numbered port. Privileged ports, or every port underneath port 1024
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=299)** are considered special ports by the Linux kernel. Apps need to be running as root in order to use them. Even though the docker engine runs as root, I've found that some systems require additional configuration to fully support low-numbered ports. To avoid these conflicts, I simply just use higher-numbered ports, and since 8080 is just repeat it twice, I used that here. However, I could've just as easily made this something else, like 1234 or 5000. It's really your choice. So we're ready to run this now, so let's run it. We get an ID back, just like we did before. Finally, let's start the container with... Let's clear our screen first. Start and then attach. Great. So now let's use Netcat again to restart our simple server. So I'm going to do nc -l -p 80. And just like before, we're waiting for input. Now that that's started, let's back to container-b and try to connect to container-a again with Telnet. So 172.18.0.2 at 80. Actually, let's cancel this, use ifconfig eth0, make sure that the IP address is the same, which it is. And then let's rerun the server and connect to it. Now, we're still hanging here. Why is that? Well, we still can't connect to this container directly as they're on different networks. However, because we published port 80 on container-a to port 80 on our host machine, we can connect to it
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=392)** via network-a's gateway IP address, or 172.18.0.1. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to cancel this. I'm going to clear my screen. One way that we can confirm the container's gateway is by running ip route. And what this will give you is the container's [[Routing]] table, and what we're looking for is this default route. The default route will always go via the [[NIC|network interface card]]'s gateway, which is just 172.18.0.1 in this case. So this is how we know that this container's gateway is set to 172.18.0.1. So we're going to go ahead and copy this since we're already here, and then we're going to clear our screen again. We're going to restart the server, and then we're going to run telnet again, but this time we're going to connect to the gateway of that bridge network at 172.18.0.1. And we'll put 8080 here instead of 80 because that's a port that's being published by container-a, mapped to port 80 within that container. So now we're connected, which is great news. So what happens if I type stuff, like, say "Hello" and "world!"? We can see that when I typed "Hello" and "world!", this container got those packets and printed the text that was in them, which means that they are now able to connect to each other. Since port 80 on container-a is mapped to port 8080 on our machine, then we should theoretically be able
>
> **[8:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=489)** to do this right from our machine. So let's go ahead and try it. To start, I'm going to type Ctrl+C then type E to get out of Telnet. Then I'm going to type Ctrl+D to log out of the container's terminal session. Once I'm back on my own machine, I'm going to clear the screen, and then I'm going to run telnet. Then instead of 172.18.0.1, we're going to put in localhost here, followed by 8080. So let's go ahead and try this. Now, you might see this error message pop up. This can happen if you try to connect to a port that's published that a container previously used, which is a little weird. So what we're going to do on container-a is simply stop the server and restart it, then retry telnet. And as you can see, we are now connected from our own computer, and if I type "Hello", "World!", we get the same results. Now remember, if you're using a Mac or [[Windows]] machine to follow along, the Docker engine is running inside of the VM. This means that these ports will be mapped to ports within your VM, not your actual machine. Docker Desktop works around this by using a background program called VPNKit to proxy connections between your host and containers within its VM. Lima does the same thing but with SSH tunnels. Additionally, you can only map to ports that are free on your host. Trying to map to taken ports will not work. So keep these in mind if you run into troubles
>
> **[9:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=583)** connecting to published ports. Let's wrap up by seeing a more practical example of port publishing in action, or perhaps I should say, revisiting. So let's get out of Telnet, and let's actually stop this container, exit, and remove this terminal. Way back in the course, we learned how to install Docker on various systems. In learning this, we also learned about Portainer, a helpful tool for interacting with just about every Docker resource, like containers and container networks. Let's bring back the command I used to start Portainer. I can do that easily by typing Ctrl+R, then portainer. Here it is. We now know that we were able to visit Portainer at localhost:9443, which we can see over here. This option right here told Docker to publish port 9443 within the Portainer container and map it to port 9443 on our host. This also makes it possible to run Portainer on any container network and still be able to reach it on port 9443. So I'm going to start this container. Here we go, and just like I did before, after I run this I should be able to go to a browser and visit [https://localhost:9443](https://localhost:9443). And after we get this warning message and click continue to visit this website,
>
> **[11:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/exposing-container-ports-between-containers?u=76281980&t=678)** we should see Portainer pop up just like it did before. So we learned how to expose a single port within a container to the outside world. What if we want to expose multiple ports without specifying -p or --publish a bunch of times? For this scenario, we need to turn on host mode networking. We'll learn about that next.

#### [Sharing a host's network with host mode networking](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sharing-a-host-s-network-with-host-mode-networking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sharing-a-host-s-network-with-host-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we know more about how the bridge network driver works, let's talk about another popular network driver, the host driver. The host driver allows containers to share the same networking stack as it's host. This is also called Host Mode Networking. Unlike the bridge driver, containers that use the host driver do not get the network name spaces unshared to them. They also do not get allocated virtual network adapters. For all intents and purposes, containers that use host mode networking get their packets like any other process on your system. As you can imagine, there are several benefits to using host mode networking, because the host driver avoids all of the IP tables, and virtual ethernet overhead required by the bridge driver, host mode networking is typically slightly faster than bridge networking, however, you likely won't notice the difference in most applications. A much bigger advantage of using host mode networking is not needing to worry about publishing ports. Because containers that use host mode networking use the host networking stack, they also gain access to the host's ports. This can be useful if your application needs to bind to many ports, though keep in mind that the limitations regarding privileged ports that we saw a few videos ago still apply. As a result, the --publish command line option is ignored when using host mode networking. Another big advantage of using host mode networking is that containers are accessible from the host's IP address instead of a quote, unquote virtual IP address
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sharing-a-host-s-network-with-host-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=93)** behind a bridge network. This is especially advantageous for applications that are sensitive to network access translation or NAT like VPN software. Let's go back into our terminal, and do the same exercise we did before with our two containers, but from within a container that's using host mode networking. So let's set up our two terminals again, and first, I'm going to run docker container rm container-a in the top terminal, so I can reuse that same container name. Since I don't need to start the container, and attach to it separately in this scenario, I'll use docker run --rm here. Now, since I want container A to use host mode networking instead of the default bridge driver, I'll type in --net=host. I'll then configure it's entry point to be [[Netcat]] or NC since that's the program I want this container to run. Finally, I'll put the image tag curlimages/curl. After this, I'll put the same arguments that I provided to Netcat last time or -l -p 8080. Note that I'm using 8080 instead of 80 here. Because we're going to use port 8080 on our actual host instead of mapping it, we'll likely get an error here if we try to use a privileged port like port 80. Okay, let's run this thing. Just like last time, it's silently awaiting input from us. So on the bottom terminal, if we were to run this Telnet program, our connection would immediately close. And the reason for this is
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sharing-a-host-s-network-with-host-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=185)** because Netcat awaits input, but in order to receive input as we learned earlier, we need to be able to have a container that supports interactive mode. This is the same problem that we experienced when we tried to create a container that runs bash as it's default entry point, but with the container set to non-interactive mode, it just exits. So to fix that, what I'm going to do is I'm going to go back up to docker run. Let's clear our screen first. I'm going to go back up to docker run, and then after net host, I'm just going to add -i here, and then I'll just run it again. Now, it's hanging, waiting for input again. So let's run Telnet down here. And as you can see, we are now connected, and it doesn't immediately close like last time. So to test it out, I'm going to type Hello like we did last time, and World like we did last time. And as you can see, container A received our input. So let's wrap up by showing how we can have container A use multiple ports. We can simply run multiple Netcat servers at once to do this. To do that, I'm going to go back to my top terminal, and stop Netcat with Ctrl+C. Next, I'm going to change my docker run command, so that I can create a shell session instead. So I'm going to add a -t after our -i, so that we can allocate a pseudo terminal, change our entry point to bash, and remove the arguments at the end after the image name. Now, this failed because this container doesn't have bash,
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/sharing-a-host-s-network-with-host-mode-networking?u=76281980&t=280)** so instead I'm just going to use SH. Once I'm inside of the container, I'm going to run Netcat on port 80. So I'm going to do -l -p 8080, and then I'm going to put an ampersand at the end so that this runs in the background. I'll do the same for ports 8081 and 8082. So now, we're waiting. So let's go back to the bottom terminal to see if this worked. Let's start with port 8080. So we're connected again. So what happens if I type Hello, we get Hello, World, same thing. So let's go ahead and Ctrl, bracket, and then type Exit or just Ctrl+C. Then we'll go back to that Telnet command, and change 8080 to 8081. This works just the same. And then 8082. Once again, worked just the same. Next, we'll learn how to disable networking entirely with the none driver.

#### [Disabling networking with the none driver](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/disabling-networking-with-the-none-driver?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/disabling-networking-with-the-none-driver?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've talked about bridge and host networking, let's go in the opposite direction and talk about disabling external networking entirely with the null network driver. The null network driver is used to create containers without any external networking capabilities. These containers will not be able to communicate with other containers on any network or with the outside world. Containers connected to a network backed with a null driver will get a loop back interface so that it can connect to itself through the special 127.0.0.1 IP address and localhost DNS label. The non-network is provided by default, but you can create your own known networks here. That's all there is to the null driver. Let's dive into a terminal and see it in action. Let's exec into a container that's connected to the non-network to see how much we can't do. To do that, I'm going to run docker run --rm --net =none --entrypoint sh curlimages/curl.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/disabling-networking-with-the-none-driver?u=76281980&t=64)** Notice the --net=none option here. That's what disables networking in this container. Okay, once I hit Enter, we'll be, well, I forgot the -i and -t so let's go ahead and put that in there and, cool, we're in. So let's first take a look at the network interfaces available in this container with ifconfig. As we can see, all we have is loopback. This means that we won't be able to communicate with anything outside of this container. For example, if I ping [[Google]]'s DNS server 8.8 8.8, I get a network unreachable error. Same thing if I try Cloud Cloudflare's DNS server at quad one or 1.1.1.1 However, because we have a loopback network driver, I can ping myself at 127.0.0.1. This distinction matters. The null network driver doesn't disable networking entirely. It only disables external networking outside of the container. This means that networked applications running within the container will still be able to talk to each other at localhost.

#### [Advanced network drivers and network plugins](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For most of the containers that you'll create with Docker, you'll likely use Bridge or Host mode networking. You might even use None to isolate some containers from everything else. However, there are rare situations where you'll need to use more advanced options. Let's learn a little more about them now. The first advanced network drivers that we'll talk about are the macvlan and ipvlan drivers. The macvlan and ipvlan drivers allow you to give your containers IP addresses on your own network. This can be useful for containers running applications that need to discover other devices on the network, like music servers, or home automation software, or secure, high-performance applications that need to bypass a network bridge without having access to all of the host's network interfaces. The macvlan and ipvlan drivers are very similar, architecturally. Both drivers work by binding containers to specific network cards on the host running Docker engine. However, there are two key differences between them to keep in mind. The first and biggest difference between them is how they handle MAC addresses. Macvlan gives each Docker container their own random MAC address. This is required in order for the container to receive an IP address on your network. However, this can quickly overwhelm your router's MAC address tables and cause your network to become slow, or stop working over time. Ipvlan works around this by giving every container the same MAC address and using a network driver on the host to handle traffic. The ipvlan driver also has modes in which it can operate.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=96)** In L2 mode, the ipvlan driver behaves almost exactly like macvlan, except everything going through a single MAC address. In L3 mode, all [[Routing]] is done by the Docker host instead of working through a network interface's default gateway. This can improve performance, but requires additional configuration. Docker does not provide networks that use the macvlan, or ipvlan, drivers by default. You'll need to use the docker network create command and specify -d macvlan, or -d ipvlan options to create one. You'll also need to specify the subnet you want containers to use with --subnet. Note that macvlan networks will need to use the same subnet as the network interfaces they are binding to. You can specify the network interface you want your network to bind to with -o, followed by the name of the interface. For example, if you wanted to use interface eth0 on your host, you would provide -o eth0. If you need to use a specific VLAN tag, use -o eth0 then a dot followed by the VLAN tag. You must use the -o option if you're using the macvlan driver or the ipvlan driver in L2 mode. Finally, macvlan networks, or L2 mode ipvlan networks, will also need to specify the gateway containers should connect through with --gateway. As you can see, this is optional
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=189)** for L3 mode ipvlan networks, since all of the networking logic is going through Docker. Both drivers handle IP address management for you, though you can still use the --ip option with docker run, or docker container create, to use a specific IP address. Let's look at our high level network diagram from before to demonstrate what these commands might look like in practice. I'm going to introduce a few networking terms we haven't covered in this course. If you're unfamiliar with some of these terms, I recommended exploring [[LinkedIn]] Learning's catalog of networking fundamentals courses. Let's say that our router has an IP address of 192.168.1.1. Every device that connects to the router will be placed into a /24 subnet with an IP address prefix of 192.168.1.0. Our host is connected to the subnet and only network card eth0 received an IP address of 192.168.1.20. We want to connect some containers to a macvlan network so that they get IP addresses from within the 192.168.1.0/24 subnet. To do that, I would start by writing, docker network create -d macvlan. Afterwards, I would put in --subnet 192.168.1.0/24 to tell Docker to allocate IP addresses from this subnet. Next, I would tell Docker to bind this network to eth0
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=283)** by typing -o eth0. Finally, I would use --gateway 192.168.1.1 to tell Docker that traffic should go through our router. Finally, I would put the network name, in this case, my-net. Docker would allocate IP addresses starting from the first available IP address within the subnet that it knows about. Since the gateway is at 192.168.1.1 and our subnet is at 192.168.1.0/24, the first IP address to use will be 192.168.1.2. Subnet masks make [[IP Addressing]] tricky sometimes. You can use the link shown here to calculate IP address ranges. After starting three containers with the docker container create command, I would then expect to see our containers get addresses 192.168.1.2, 3, and 4, respectfully. Now, let's say that I replaced the host ethernet cable after one of my cats ate through it. After doing that, our host got a new IP address of 192.168.1.2. We already have a container with that IP address, but the router might not know that. If this happens, network performance on both addresses will drop substantially. Fortunately, we can tell Docker to not allocate 192.168.1.2 in two ways. First, we can use the --aux-address, or A-U-X address, to give Docker a list of IP addresses
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=377)** to exclude from allocation. Each option requires a label of the host, or device being excluded, and its IP address separated with an equal sign. In our case, we would use --aux-address host=192.168.1.2
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=394)** to prevent the IP address conflict. You can specify aux-address multiple times for every address you want to exclude. And as you can see up here, instead of our first container getting 192.168.1.2, it gets the next available IP address, or 192.168.1.3. You can also specify an IP address range with the --ip-range option. This tells Docker to allocate IP addresses from, you guessed it, a range of addresses. You need to provide the range in CODR format, just like our subnet. So for example, let's say you wanted to reserve at least 50 IP addresses for this network that do not include our host's network address. This slide shows one way of doing that. Here, we're reserving the block 192.168.1.64/26, which will give us 64 addresses to work with. Since the first usable address in this range is 192.168.1.64, our containers will have IP addresses of 192.168.1.64 through 67, respectively. You need to be careful about creating a range that has IP addresses that are taken. You can configure many routers to reserve a range of IP addresses to avoid this. Another advanced network driver that a Docker provides is the overlay network driver. This driver allows you to create a virtual network across two or more nodes running the Docker engine, or a Swarm.
>
> **[8:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=489)** Swarms are provided by Docker Swarm, Swarm's official container orchestrator. Swarm allows you to create and manage containers across multiple machines. Since we don't cover Swarm in this course, we won't spend too much time on the overlay driver. However, since every container orchestrator uses an overlay network of some kind, we'll spend a few minutes talking about how they work. Check out the link shown here if you're interested in learning more about how to create a network that uses the overlay driver. This diagram is an example of an overlay network created by flannel, a popular container networking fabric. The goal of an overlay network is to make containers running on multiple machines, and potentially multiple networks, appear as if they are on a single network. To do this, most overlays use three things; a network tunnel, shown under circle 1, virtual network interfaces shown under circles 2 and agents running on hosts shown under circles 3. The agents are responsible for sending network packets from containers through virtual network interfaces installed by the overlay. The virtual network interfaces then wrap each packet into a bigger packet that gets sent through the tunnel to its destination. Usually, the packets are encrypted in transit within the tunnel to prevent outsiders from figuring out what's being sent within it, or through it. Once it arrives there, the original packet is retrieved from the bigger packet and sent to the container through the overlay's virtual network interface. There are several technologies that overlays use to establish the secure tunnel, Virtual Extensible LAN,
>
> **[9:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/advanced-network-drivers-and-network-plugins?u=76281980&t=584)** or vxlan, and Wire Guard are the two most popular choices. Since Docker's network driver system is pluggable, you can install and use third party network drivers through network plugins. Network plugins can be found either on Docker Hub or through [[GitHub]]. Weaveworks Weave Net plugin is the most popular network plugin. Weave Net allows you to create virtual networks for containers that span multiple hosts, just like Docker's overlay driver. Weave Net also comes with additional features not provided by the default network drivers, like service discovery and network policies. You can learn more about Weave Net at this link shown here.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [Introduction to container storage](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-container-storage?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-container-storage?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've learned quite a bit about containers so far. Up until now, we've seen how to put applications into container images and start containers from them. We also learned about how container run times use control groups and namespaces to isolate these apps in their environments from each other. We even learned how to create Docker containers within Docker containers. How cool is that? However, there's one thing that almost every app has in common that we haven't talked about. At some point, most apps will need to store data somewhere. What happens then? You might recall from our container image deep dives that the file systems presented to containers are an overlay file system behind the scenes. These overlay file systems combine the read only files within the layers of the container's image, and a read/write layer into a single unified system for the container to use. However, union file systems don't last forever. When the container goes away, the file system goes with it. This isn't a big deal for applications that don't rely on local storage to operate. At worst, they'll attempt to reconnect to their backing stores, and fail if they are unable to do so. However, this is a massive deal for applications that do. It would be a really bad time if a database that you or your company relied on restarted and lost all of its data. Fortunately, Docker has a great solution that avoids this catastrophe. It's called Volumes, and in this chapter we are going to learn all about them. We'll learn what volumes are
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-container-storage?u=76281980&t=93)** and how volume drivers create them. We'll learn how to create volumes and mount them to our own containers. We'll also learn how to mount folders on our system to containers through bind mounts, and the trade-offs that come with that. Finally, we'll learn how to easily get data into and out of volume.

#### [How volumes work](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/how-volumes-work?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/how-volumes-work?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Volumes allow you to safely handle data coming into and out of containers. More importantly, they allow you to save data from your containers so that you can worry less about containers rebooting or becoming otherwise unavailable during your app's lifecycle. Let's learn more about them. We learned that file systems resented to containers are actually unified sets of folders behind the scenes. Volumes work similarly, when a volume is mounted into a container, Docker transparently maps a folder into a mount point within the container. How this folder is created and presented to your container, is managed by a volume driver. Much like the network and storage driver we learned about previously, volume drivers define how volumes are created and managed by containers. By default, Docker uses the local volume driver. The local driver simply creates a directory within Docker system directory, and mounts it into the container like a virtual disk of sorts. Like network or storage drivers, you can install volume drivers to create many different kinds of volumes. Docker maintains a quote unquote, official list of volume drivers on their website at the link shown on your screen. Unfortunately, almost all of them are either unmaintained or considered quote unquote legacy by their owners. Fortunately, everything you'll need to do with volumes can be done with the local volume driver or by mounts, which we'll cover soon. One last thing about the local driver before we move on. Despite its name, the local driver can actually mount NFS and SIFs slash samba file shares.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/how-volumes-work?u=76281980&t=97)** You can do this by specifying type equals NFS or type equals CIFS or SIFs to the dash dash volume mount option when creating the volume, which we'll learn about in the next video. You can also use type equals device to mount block devices into containers with the local driver. In an earlier video, we mounted slash DEV slash VDA one within our VM to a container by sending the container to privilege mode before starting it. This is a safer way of doing that. However, this shouldn't be something you need to do often. Check out Docker's documentation on volumes here if you're interested in learning more about these options.

#### [Creating Docker volumes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-volumes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-volumes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Volumes are created with the docker volume create command. This command is straightforward. Simply provide the name of your volume and the driver to use with the -d option if needed. Your volume driver might have additional configuration options. If so, use the -o flag to specify them. Linking a volume to a container is similarly straightforward, but with a slight twist. There are two ways to mount a volume to your container. The short way with --volume, or -v for short, or the long way with --mount. Let's take a look at them now. Let's start with the long way.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-volumes?u=76281980&t=40)** - -mount allows you to specify exactly how Docker should mount your volume. It does so by accepting five key value pairs separated by commas. The type sub-option shown here specifies the type of mount that we're creating. There are three types that Docker supports: bind, tnpfs, spelled T N P F S, and volume. Since we'll cover bind mounts later, let's assume this will always be volume for now. The source sub-option specifies the name of the volume that is being mounted into the container. This is the name that you would provide to the docker volume create command we saw earlier. The destination sub-option tells Docker where to mount the volume to within the container. This can be any directory. If it doesn't exist within the container's image, Docker will create it. You can provide ro, or readonly, to mark the volume as read only. This is useful when you want to mount data like passwords or configuration files that the container shouldn't be able to modify. Finally, you can provide options to the volume driver for this specific volume with the volume-opt key. This can be specified more than once and the values that you provide can vary from driver to driver. Type, source, and destination are mandatory when using mount. Read only and volume-opt are not. The -v option is much simpler. It takes three arguments separated by colons. The name of the volume, or source, the folder into which the volume will be mounted
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-volumes?u=76281980&t=133)** within the container, or the destination, and the ro/readonly and volume-opt options we saw above. You'll usually use the -v form to mount volumes as it is much easier to write. However, knowing that both [[Forms]] exist is important especially if you use a volume driver plugin. Now that we know more about how volumes work, let's jump into a terminal to practice our new skills. Let's say that we're creating a container that's going to save some data that we can't afford to lose. This is a good use case for creating a volume backed by the local driver, so let's go ahead and do that. I'll start by typing docker volume create. I don't need to provide a -d here since Docker will create local volumes by default. Next, I'll give this volume a name. Given the data we're dealing with, let's call it super-duper-important, and hit enter. We know that this command worked because Docker gave us the name of the volume back. Let's create our super important container. To do that, I'll run docker run, and since I'm going to exec into this container after I create it, I'll add the -i and -t commands. I also don't need this container around after I'm done with it, so I'll add --rm to it as well. We're now ready to link our volume to this container. I'll start by going the long way with --mount followed by a single quote. We first need the type. Since this is a volume, we'll put type, and then an equal sign, and then volume here. Next, we'll need to tell Docker
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-docker-volumes?u=76281980&t=226)** where to mount our volume into with the source and destination keys. Our source is going to be the volume that we just created, or super duper important. The destination, well, since that can be anything, let's just go ahead and make it /super-important. To recap, the command that we're going to run is docker run

#### [Creating bind mounts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-bind-mounts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-bind-mounts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've learned about Docker volumes, let's talk about another way of bringing data into your containers, the bind mount. Let's imagine a common scenario. Your code has a set of tests that resides in a directory. Since your Apple ship has a container image, you want to run these tests within a container without rebuilding your image every time. How would you go about this? One way would be to copy our code into a volume. To do that, first we would need to create the volume with docker volume create and then the volume name or foo in this case. Then we would need to create a container that maps that volume into a directory within the container. We can do that with docker container create -v foo and then map that to /app and then we can use the -w flag to set app as the working directory for our container called ctr. Then we would need to use docker cp to copy our directory into our container at /app. So we would do docker cp, the directory containing our tests, and then ctr:/app. And then once we're done, we would remove the container. This is fine, but this would get tedious after a while. Bind mounts solve this problem. With bind mounts, all you need to do is map your source code directory to a folder within a container and run your tests. No volumes to manage and no leftover containers to deal with. Simply put, bind mounts map directories on your computer to directories into your containers.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-bind-mounts?u=76281980&t=94)** There are a few other use cases where I personally like bind mounts. I'm sure that this isn't an exhaustive list, though. I like using bind mounts for hot reloading changes to a running application. This is popular with front-end developers who want to see their websites change as they make modifications to components or static site generators like Hugo that render pages as you change content. I also like using bind mounts for backing up and restoring Docker volumes. Since we'll see this in action in the next video, I won't spoil the fun here. Bind mounts have also been useful for running applications in one-off containers, like running [[SQL]] to perform actions against a [[PostgreSQL|Postgres]] database that isn't in a volume. Just like regular volumes, we can bind mount the long way or the short way with docker run --mount or docker run -v. Let's start the long way. The set of arguments to --mount looks almost identical to that of a volume. Let's explain each argument in the same order. The --type indicates the type of mount that we're creating. Last time, this was set to volume to create a volume. This time, we're going to set it to bind for bind mounts. Next up is source. Source indicates the directory on your computer that you're mounting into the container. In our example from earlier, this would be the directory directory. Destination indicates the directory within the container into which the source directory will be mounted. Again, from our example earlier, this would be ./app. The directory does not need to exist beforehand.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-bind-mounts?u=76281980&t=187)** Docker will create it for you automatically. Just like volumes, you can optionally provide the read-only option to mount if you want to make this bind mount read-only. Finally, bind mounts accept two advanced configuration parameters: bind-propagation and selinux-label. Bind propagation tells [[Linux]] how mounts within the mount or sub-mounts are handled internally by Linux. Since this is quite complicated and beyond the scope of this course, I recommend checking out this link if you're interested in learning more about bind mount propagation. selinux-label specifies whether the directory being mounted can be shared with other containers. It must be colon lowercase z or zed for mounts that will be shared with other containers or colon capital Z or capital Zed for mounts that will not. Check out this link if you are interested in learning more about this feature. While the long way is useful for [[Fine Tuning]] your bind mount, you'll usually want to use the short way here. The notation is the same as mounting a volume, source directory first, then a colon, then the destination directory. If you want to make the mount read-only, put a colon ro at the end. When you're using the short way on some systems, the source directory might need to be an absolute directory. In other words, in those situations, you'll need to provide the full path to the directory you're mounting into the container. For example, us putting ./directory in those situations will not work. Instead, we'll need to put the full path
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-bind-mounts?u=76281980&t=280)** to the directory directory. Another thing to keep in mind, if you try to mount a source directory that does not exist using the short way, Docker will automatically create a directory for you on your computer. This usually bites people when they try to mount specific files into a container that doesn't exist beforehand. When they try to use files within the container, they're often surprised that the file they mounted is actually a directory. We'll see this when we explore bind mounting in a terminal. This is a good time to experiment, so let's dig deeper into bind mounting. To begin, I'm going to create a directory inside of the /tmp/lima directory called stuff. I'm going to use the -p parameter to make sure that M-K-D-I-R or mkdir creates the entire directory structure. I.e. if this Lima directory doesn't exist, it will create that as well. Great, so now we're going to put stuff into stuff. Namely, I'm going to create 10 files inside of stuff, each called file hyphen, followed by a number from one to 10. All of them will contain the line, this is file number, dollar x or where is the file's number. To do that, I'm going to type this really long command and then run it. Cool, if we ls /tmp/lima/stuff, we can see that the files have been created. So if I cat say /tmp/lima/stuff/file-3, we get this as file number three, perfect.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-bind-mounts?u=76281980&t=374)** Let's use bind mounting to view these files within a container without creating a volume. To do that, I'm going to create and attach to a temporary container with docker run -rm. Then I'm going to use -v /tmp/lima/stuff and mount it into the /stuff folder within the container. Then with the alpine image, I'm going to attempt a cat /stuff/file-3. So that worked and that also worked because we provided an absolute path here. Now, let's say for example, that this was not in /tmp/lima, but let's say this was in our current directory with the dot. Now, this might fail on some systems because some systems, like we said earlier, require this path to be an absolute path. So in that situation, you have two options. You can either provide $PWD to put the full path to your current working directory before you specify /stuff, or you can use a tool called realpath followed by the path to your stuff directory, which in this case is just the current directory. Now, you might not have realpath installed, so if you don't have it installed, you can run brew install coreutils on the Mac to install it. Linux usually comes with it, so this shouldn't be an issue for my Linux and [[Windows]] users. What happens if you try to add a new file to our mount from within the container?
>
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-bind-mounts?u=76281980&t=467)** So what do I mean by this? So going back to docker run --rm -v /tmp/lima/stuff into stuff alpine, what happens if I tried to do something like echo, this is file number 10, and then redirected that to /stuff/file-10? Now, because I'm using the redirection operator here, I'm expecting this string "This is file number 10" to get written into a file called file-10 inside of stuff, but /stuff is mounted to /tmp/lima/stuff. So what I would expect to have happen here is that this directory would contain file number 10. Is that what actually happens? Let's find out. So I'm going to go ahead and run this and it looks like I made a mistake and forgot to include sh here. So let's go back here and type sh -c and there we go. That worked. So did that file get written into /tmp/lima/stuff? /tmp/lima/stuff/file-10, yes it did. So there you go, that's how buying mounting works in a nutshell.

#### [Copying data from volumes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/copying-data-from-volumes?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/copying-data-from-volumes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Copying data from volumes is pretty easy to do, though it's a little unconventional at first. For example, some drivers might store data in an NFS volume or some form of object storage that you won't easily have access to. #### [Bind mount caveats with Docker Desktop](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/bind-mount-caveats-with-docker-desktop?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/bind-mount-caveats-with-docker-desktop?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Bind mounts are powerful. Being able to read and write to directories you own inside of containers makes lots of tasks easy within Docker. However, there is one gotcha to be aware of when you're running Docker outside of [[Linux]]. Performance. Let's say that you had an app that was writing gigabyte-size files several times during its lifetime. Maybe these are logs, or results, or an API query. I'm going to use a tool called DD to simulate that within a container. We'll use DD to write a random file that's a gigabyte wide. So, let's go ahead and type "docker run --rm." I'm going to mount the "super-duper-important" volume that we created earlier into a directory called "/app." Next, using the "alpine" image, I'm going to type "dd" to read a file system with "if=/dev/urandom," and write that data to a file called "/app/stuff.txt." And I'll tell DD that's where I want to write to with "of=." I want this file to be a gigabyte wide, so I'll type in "bs," which is short for block sector, "=1G." And since DD will write to the file indefinitely, I'll tell it to only read one block with "count=1." So, just as a note, DD is a super useful tool. So check out this link if you want to learn more about it. Now, just for fun, I want to record how long this is going to take so, to do that, I'm going to slat "time" right to the front of this command, right, so right before "docker." This looks good, so let's run it.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/bind-mount-caveats-with-docker-desktop?u=76281980&t=96)** So we can see that that took a little while, but what happens if we try to do this with a bind mount? Let's go back to this command, and let's remove "super-duper-important," and let's replace it with the {indistinct} stuff directory that we created earlier, "/tmp/lima/stuff." Let's do it. Look at the difference in times. This took almost a minute, versus 15 seconds before. Why was this so much slower? Well, the magic of bind mounting our working directory comes at a cost. Since Docker is running within a Linux VM, your working directory will most likely not be native to the machine. In almost every case, these directories are shared with the virtual machine and are made to appear as if they are native to the machine behind the scenes. This means that every read and right to your bind mount needs to pass through some kind of a driver before that IO hits your host. This slowness can get very painful when working with large files or lots of small files. The main takeaway here is to use bind mounts with caution. If you're running Docker on Linux, or if your app isn't very data intensive, or if you don't mind waiting a little longer for stuff to finish, then bind mounts are great. Otherwise, I would recommend copying your data into volumes or rebuilding your container image on save.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **CLI Commands:** docker (6), rm (1)
> **Definitions:** short for (1), is a  (1), means that (1)
> **Warnings:** gotcha (1), be aware (1), caution (1)
> **File Paths:** app/stuff.txt (1)
> **Env Vars:** api (1)
> **Cross-References:** go back to (1)
