### 9. The Docker Registry

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Introduction to Docker registries](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-docker-registries?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-docker-registries?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We spent a lot of time learning about container internals. Let's turn our attention to one of Docker's most powerful features, the Container Registry. Up until this point, we've been building our images locally, but what if we wanted to share those images with other people? Take the my-app image we created earlier, for example. Let's say that I had a friend who was interested in using our application as a container, but doesn't want to create it themselves from source. I could save my image with Docker Save, to a file called image.tar. Then once I get it to my friend somehow, they could run docker load ./image.tar my-app, to save the image into Docker's Image Store, and then create containers from it with Docker run. As you can imagine, there are a lot of problems with this approach. The first problem is getting them the image. This image is pretty small, so I could probably email it to them just fine, assuming that their email server doesn't block attachments. But what if this image was 10 gigabytes big? Then I'd have to use something like [[Google Drive]], [[Microsoft OneDrive|OneDrive]], or Dropbox, all of which add friction towards getting this app to my friend. The second problem happens after they get the image. How do they know that they're getting my my-app image, and not some version of my-app that was mutated in transit to contain a crypto miner? Also, while I could tag my image with the app's version, there's really no guarantee that they're getting that version since it's just a tag. The third problem is Docker itself. It would be a real pain if we had to docker save
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-docker-registries?u=76281980&t=94)** and docker load every time we wanted to use a third party image. At that point, we might as well just use floppy disks. Enter the Container Image Registry. The Image Registry is a web server that serves container images, that's it, that's all it does. Some registries are fancier than others, but at its core, it's a simple web server. What sets it apart from a regular web server is how the images are laid out. Registries use a particular folder structure to make it easy for Docker to find images and verify that they are who they claim to be. The beauty of the Image Registry is that it works seamlessly with Docker pull. As long as you are logged into the registry, you can simply run Docker pull my-app and Docker will retrieve it from the registry like a librarian retrieves a book from a massive set of bookshelves. No more docker load and no more docker save. Docker Hub is by far the most popular Container Image Registry out there. Docker hub is run by the Docker Project themselves, and is also the default image registry used by the Docker client. However, an essential skill of working with containers, is learning how to use other registries. Many registries you'll come across are on [[GitHub]], AWS, [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]], and others. Many of them will also be private. Some might even have special authentication requirements that are beyond the scope of what Docker Hub can provide. In this chapter, we'll learn almost everything you need to know about registries.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/introduction-to-docker-registries?u=76281980&t=188)** We'll create our own registry and load my image into it to explore how images are laid out within a registry. We'll also learn how to push multi-platform image manifest lists to make pulling a multi-platform container image seamless. Finally, we'll learn of popular image registries you might come across as you gain experience with containers.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [Creating a simple registry](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-a-simple-registry?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-a-simple-registry?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we know a little more about container registries, let's create our own. There are two ways to create a registry. You can either run the registry as a container from Docker's official image, or you can front the registry with a reverse proxy. We're going to learn how to do both, and when to do one or the other. So, let's start with using Docker's official image. First, we're going to want to create the container that will run the registry. We can do that with "docker run --rm," and let's go ahead and call it "registry." Since the registry runs on Port 5000 by default, we need to configure the container to map that port onto our machine. We can easily do this with "-p 5000:5000." Remember: outside, colon, inside. This is pretty anti-climactic because both ports are the same. But if I were to use like 5001, the outside port would be 5001 and the inside port would be 5000. But let's go ahead and just have them be both 5000 here. Since the container will emit a lot of logs as it runs, I'm going to use the "-d" option to make this container run in the background. Finally, we need the name of the registry image, and, in this case, it's just going to be called "registry:2." Why 2? Well, it just so happens that there are two versions of the registry as of the time of this recording, v1 and v2. And as you can see, our app still works, but before that, it actually went ahead and attempted to pull these layers from our registry. Now, because these layers already worked it didn't have to do any pulls. But, if they didn't exist, you would see them pulling like we did when we pulled the Nginx image

#### [Creating and logging into authenticated registries](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-and-logging-into-authenticated-registries?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-and-logging-into-authenticated-registries?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Earlier we learned how to create a simple registry. However, most of the non Docker hub registries that you'll work with are not created like this for a few reasons. First, simple registries aren't secure at all. As you saw we didn't need any credentials to log into the registry. This would be a problem, if you had a really sensitive container image, in the registry and it was connected to the internet. Additionally, since we connected to it with http, a curious beaver wanting to listen into our traffic could see the images we're uploading, and downloading into the registry. This would be bad, if we were working on something sensitive even if it wasn't going to live in a public registry. Simple registries aren't very configurable. What if we wanted to simulate a low balance registry, or a registry with some custom authentication mechanism? The registry two image doesn't provide much to accommodate these wants. Fortunately, these problems are easily solved, by putting a reverse proxy in front of our registry. A reverse proxy is a web server, that internally intercepts and forwards traffic it receives, to a destination that's usually an internal device. It is the opposite of a regular proxy that externally intercepts and forwards the packets it receives before sending it through to some other network. The illustration here, demonstrates how this works. A regular proxy, like the one you might have at work, acts as a middleman between you and some other network. Usually the internet. When you go to [linkedin.com/learning](https://linkedin.com/learning), in your browser, the packet sent between you, and [[LinkedIn]] learning will be sent through the proxy.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-and-logging-into-authenticated-registries?u=76281980&t=95)** Here, the proxy determines whether to send the packets through, rewrite them beforehand, or simply drop them. A reverse proxy, like the one we're going to set up, will usually forward your request to some other address. This way when we type [https://registry:5000](https://registry:5000), it internally goes to some other address, like registry backend:9999, with you being none the wiser. So, let's talk about what we're going to do. We're going to use Engine X, a very popular web server to create this reverse proxy. First, we'll create a bridge network that will host our simple registry, as well as our reverse proxy. Next, we're going to create a self-signed certificate, with open SSL, an all purpose SSL toolkit. We'll also add the quote unquote certificate authority certificate that this will create to our system to avoid insecure connection errors. Then we're going to create our registry container, just like we did in the last video. After that, we're going to configure our web server to proxy, all of the traffic it receives to our registry container. We will also configure these connections to use certificates, and then finally, we will create the web server, and try to log into it with Docker Login. One caveat before we proceed, if you joined me in creating and pushing manifest lists in the last video, note that they do not work with local secured registries. This is due to a limitation with the Docker registry app. Manifest lists do work with secure registries outside of your machine, however, like the [[GitHub]] container registries,

> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-and-logging-into-authenticated-registries?u=76281980&t=189)** or Artifactory, or stuff like that. First, we'll create a network called registry. Specifically we'll create a bridge network called registry. This will make it possible for our reverse proxy to find our registry server with DNS. We talked about why that is, in the networking chapter, in case you missed it. Next, we'll create the certificates, run create unsigned certs.sh, to do that. This script will run a program called Open SSL, within a container that will generate our self-signed certs. Feel free to open and explore the script, if you're interested in learning more about this process. After the script finishes, we'll see a folder called certs. In that certs you'll see two files, cert.pem and key.pem. We now need to add cert.pem into our [[Virtual Machines]] list of root certificate authorities. Certificate root authorities are special certificates used by just about every app that talks HTTPS, for verifying client and service certificates, without doing this step, we'll get weird insecure certificate errors, when we try to log into our registry.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/creating-and-logging-into-authenticated-registries?u=76281980&t=281)** Note that these steps can be used for any kind of root certificate, not just self-signed ones. I do this all the time with clients or customers, who have their own certificate authorities. To do this, let's SSH into our VM, by doing limactl, shell Docker, or if you're on [[Windows]], running Bash or Terminal and then dropping down in selecting [[Ubuntu]], or whatever, the next distribution you are using. I'm going to clear my screen. First, I'm going to copy cert cert.pem into slash usr slash local slash share slash ca hyphen certificates as registry dot CRT. The certificate must end in dot CRT, in order for the next step to work. Now, you might get this if you're not root, so if you do, just put sudu behind it. Do not copy key.pem. We'll use that in our reverse proxy, later. Now I'm going to run a script that updates the root certificates. That script happens to be called update ca certificates. You'll need to put sudu beforehand, if you're not root. After this runs, make sure that it says one added zero removed, or at least it says one added, towards the bottom of the command's output. If it doesn't say that, then one of two things probably happened. Either you didn't end your certificate, with dot CRT, or you put it in the wrong folder. Don't feel bad, I mess this up all the time. #### [Popular alternative container image registries](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/popular-alternative-container-image-registries?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/popular-alternative-container-image-registries?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've seen how to create our own registry and push images into it, let's learn about some other container registries you might use or encounter in your Docker journey. There are three main reasons why teams and organizations choose to use a registry other than the Docker Hub. The biggest reason is for increased security and [[Privacy]]. Many of the containers that you'll create for yourself or your company might contain private or sensitive information, like passwords and references to internal servers. It would be really bad if we could only store that data within Docker Hub where everyone can see it. Private registries allow you to lock down who can access your registry or run registries within your own network away from the internet. Another big reason is to work around Docker Hub's pull rate limit. In 2020, Docker instituted a policy whereby users can pull images at most 100 times per every six-hour block. This might seem like a lot, but this limit is incredibly easy to blow through if you're not careful. Private registries work around this by letting you have control over pull rate limits. The final reason is environment compatibility. Some teams might block access to the Docker Hub or need to run containers that are completely isolated from the internet. This would make the Docker Hub a complete non-starter for them. Private registries provide a workaround in these circumstances. The popular alternate container registries can be grouped into three categories: registries provided by source-code-hosting platforms or source-code-hosting registries,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/popular-alternative-container-image-registries?u=76281980&t=95)** registries provided by cloud providers, and enterprise registries sold by vendors. Let's look at the examples, advantages, and disadvantages of each one. Source code registries are registries provided by source-code-hosting platforms, like [[GitHub]] and GitLab. GitHub's container registry is shown on this slide. Their main purpose is to make it really easy to publish container images for projects hosted on these platforms. This way, instead of writing a script that pushes an image created by your project and incorporating it into your release pipelines, you can use built-in tools provided by the hosting platform. The biggest advantage of these platforms is that they're usually free up to a certain amount of data. Of course, this means that you might need to add some automation to your workflow to prevent unnecessary changes. Source code registries integrate really well into your projects, and they're usually really easy to work with. However, unlike the Docker Hub, they require you to have a project on their platform before you can use them. GitHub and GitLab's container registries are the most popular source code registry offerings. Unfortunately, [[Atlassian]] [[Bitbucket]] doesn't currently have a hosted registry offering. Cloud provider registries, like AWS Elastic Container Registry shown here, are meant to provide an easy way to make container images available to other services offered by the cloud provider. Most of these options allow you to create registries without any services,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/popular-alternative-container-image-registries?u=76281980&t=186)** but they really shine when you integrate them into other services that they offer. Most of these providers offer free tier of some kind. These registries are usually free within the free tier. However, they're often not free once the free tier expires, so you'll need to be on top of your monthly spend to avoid a surprise bill. These registries integrate well into other services offered by the provider. However, you'll need to create an account with them before you can use them. Most of these providers require a credit card and a physical address before you can create an account with them. Finally, cloud provider registries are usually easy to administer from their UIs. The only slight disadvantage is that obtaining credentials to log into the registry with Docker Login can be awkward, as they usually don't map usernames and passwords, like source code registries do. Here are some examples of popular cloud provider registries. This list isn't exhaustive, as there are many cloud providers, and almost all of them offer hosted registries. Finally, we have enterprise registries, like the VMware Harbor Registry shown here. These are great for companies that need a registry that works within their network and environments. However, that flexibility comes at a price. If you need a registry that can work in just about any environment, you want an enterprise registry. Enterprise registries can run in the vendor's cloud or on-premise in your data center. They're also built to comply with many industry standards and regulations, which makes them an easy sell with security and compliance departments.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/popular-alternative-container-image-registries?u=76281980&t=279)** Finally, they often come with additional tools to help simplify image management at scale, like image scanning, authentication provider integration, and automatic image [[Storage Management]]. However, these features are not free. In fact, they can get quite expensive to purchase on their own. If you're installing them on-premise, they can also be challenging to install depending on the environment that we'll operate in. Lastly, here are some examples of enterprise registries that you can use online or purchase for your business. You will almost certainly run into one of these if you are using containers at a big company.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[GitHub]] (3), [[Privacy]] (1), [[Atlassian]] (1), [[Bitbucket]] (1), [[Storage Management]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (9), make (3), aws (1)
> **Tools:** github (3), gitlab (2), bitbucket (1)
> **Exercise Files:** source code (4)
> **Prerequisites:** you'll need (2), install (1)
> **Env Vars:** aws (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Docker best practices](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-best-practices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-best-practices?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Docker is awesome. In my experience, it has made writing software for multiple systems and platforms a breeze. I hope that you enjoyed learning all about Docker and how to use it. Now that you're ready to move your apps to Docker, there are three best practices I always recommend considering. First, always use verified images from Docker Hub or from a private registry. You're probably going to be downloading a bunch of images from a registry, whether that's Docker Hub or a private registry. While this is very flexible and makes using Docker very easy, it is also very easy to download images that cause harm to your systems or business. This is especially true if you're using these images as a base for creating your own images. There are many examples of developers getting hacked or having their credentials stolen from using malicious Docker images. Trend Micro reported an interesting example of this in 2020. An image called alpine2 was uploaded into the Docker Hub. Since Alpine is a very popular Docker image, many developers were confused and downloaded the alpine2 image instead. Unfortunately, while alpine2 indeed ran Alpine [[Linux]], it also configured containers to run a crypto miner on startup. If such a container ran on a platform that charges you for outbound network traffic like AWS or [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]], this will become a very expensive mistake. One way to avoid this is to always use Docker Hub images that are verified. Verified images have an official Docker image designation on them in Docker Hub to let you know that they're scanned and vetted by Docker themselves.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-best-practices?u=76281980&t=93)** Private registries like Artifactory also offer something similar. The [[Ubuntu]] image shown here is a great example of that. It is very easy to see its designation on Docker Hub. Unfortunately, there are a ton of safe images that are not verified out there. How do you know which are safe and which aren't? Fortunately, tools called image scanners solve this problem. Container image-scanning tools inspect each layer of Docker images and warn you about layers that are known to be malicious, as well as layers that have files in them that it might be harmful. Clair, Trivy, and Dagda are some examples of open-source image scanners that you can use for free. Paid options exist as well. The next best practice is to avoid tagging images as latest. We learned that Docker images will have a version tag of latest by default if one is not provided after running docker build. While this is mostly fine for local development, this can be a problem for images that will be uploaded into a registry for three reasons. First, you don't know which version of an application you're going to be getting when you download an image that is tagged with latest. Second, the version can change out from under you when you pull the image again in the future. It would be really unfortunate to have an application in production break because a version of a dependency changed without you knowing. Finally, version tags can be overwritten when you push them into the Docker Hub. This makes rolling back to previous versions difficult if you only use latest as a version tag. For these reasons, I always recommend putting a version tag when creating images, even if they are local.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/docker-best-practices?u=76281980&t=185)** It is easy, quick, and solves a lot of problems in the future. The next best practice is to use non-root users when running containers. We learned that Docker containers run as root by default. This makes security teams nervous and gives applications the ability to cause a lot of harm, both within the container and outside of it. To avoid this, I recommend running containers as a user other than root. You can do this for your own images as well as for containers created from existing images. For containers created from other images, specify the --user option when running docker Run or docker container create. You can use Linux user IDs or actual names. This can cause problems with images whose applications assume they're running as root. You might need to write additional automation to work around that. We also learned how to use capabilities to further restrict what containers can or cannot do so I would recommend looking into that as well. For your own images, you can specify a user command within your Dockerfile to tell Docker which user to run your application as by default. You can make certain sections of your Dockerfile run as root and others run as non-root. It's quite flexible.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Best Practices:** best practice (2)

#### [Taking it to the next level with Docker Compose](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/taking-it-to-the-next-level-with-docker-compose?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/taking-it-to-the-next-level-with-docker-compose?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As we've seen, Docker works really well for running single applications. But what happens when you have an application that relies on multiple components that are running separately? Here's a typical example. You have a web application that relies on APIs or backend services on an application server. The application server stores stuff in a database, which is used by APIs to return information back to the web app. You would like to deploy all of these systems as Docker containers to make it easier to test and scale your app. At first, you might be thinking, "What if I just roll them all into a single Docker image?" The problem with this is that Docker containers are really meant to run one application at a time. While you could use a process supervisor, like we saw earlier, to run multiple applications within a container, you run the risk of losing data or experiencing unpredictable behavior. "Okay, so if I can't run them all in a single container, what if I just start multiple containers and connect them each other?" It might be your next thought. And that's a good one. We learn how to do that by talking about bridge and host networks, and we also learned how to use volumes to store data between containers and share them amongst each other. So this is a great way to implement a three-tier architecture, like the one that we saw earlier. However, having to run tons of Docker [[CLI]] commands to make all of this work and get tedious and confusing. Fortunately, Docker [[Jetpack Compose|Compose]] is here to help. Docker Compose is a tool provided by Docker that makes it really easy to run and connect multiple containers on a single machine. With Compose, you can use a single file called the Compose Manifest
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/taking-it-to-the-next-level-with-docker-compose?u=76281980&t=92)** to define all of your containers and how they relate to each other. Starting those containers is as easy as running Docker Compose up. I love using Docker Compose for quickly integrating entire app infrastructures locally. For me, this has been really helpful for running integration or end-to-end tests without having to depend on the network, which can be unreliable, or having to run a ton of Docker CLI commands, which can get really tedious and burdensome. Check out docs.[docker.com/compose](https://docker.com/compose) if you're interested in getting started with it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Jetpack Compose|Compose]] (7), [[CLI]] (2)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (12), make (2)
> **Env Vars:** cli (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **URLs:** [docker.com](https://docker.com) (1)
> **Prerequisites:** getting started (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Level up even more with Kubernetes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/level-up-even-more-with-kubernetes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/level-up-even-more-with-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Docker makes it easy to run containers in a single system. Unfortunately, things get complicated really quickly once you're running hundreds or thousands of containers in production. While you can use overlay networks to connect multiple Docker engine notes together, that's only supported by swarm and it's difficult to set up otherwise. You can also use the Docker [[CLI]] to talk to Docker engines running on remote hosts but this is quite cumbersome, especially when authentication comes into play. Docker also does not have built-in solutions for moving containers between hosts or auto-scaling containers to respond to load. Finally, higher level concerns like securing traffic between containers or configuring low balancing and [[Routing]] amongst them are outside of Docker's realm of responsibility. At best, this can make using Docker alone for production type workloads really, really complex. At worst, it can increase [[Security Risk]], decrease performance, and make your infrastructure more susceptible to downtime. Container orchestrators solve these problems. Orchestrators use scheduling techniques, networking magic, and service discovery tools to make scaling, moving, and routing traffic to containers really, really easy. If you've ever used VMware's vCenter or tools like Rundeck, you're already familiar with this idea. Many container orchestrators have popped up over the years. Dockers own swarm product, D2iQ which was formally known as Mesosphere, HashiCorp Nomad, and cloud offerings like AWS Elastic Container Service are examples of some popular container orchestrators in the wild. However, the most popular container orchestrator
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/level-up-even-more-with-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=93)** in the market today is the [[Kubernetes]] project. Let's talk a little more about it. Kubernetes described itself as a planet scale container orchestrator for automating the deployment, scaling, and management of containerized applications. There are a number of features that Kubernetes provides to make this possible. First, Kubernetes is a distributed system. It is designed from the ground up to run its components and store their data across many machines. This makes it capable of running and connecting hundreds of thousands of containers seamlessly as if they were all running on one machine. This also makes Kubernetes possible to run on almost anything from Raspberry Pi to some of the largest cloud platforms in the world. Second, Kubernetes makes it really easy to group containers together, kind of like Docker [[Jetpack Compose|Compose]]. You can also use Kubernetes to scale those container groups up or down to respond to your application's demands without creating more VMs or other [[Hardware]]. This is typically expensive and sometimes cumbersome but much cheaper to do with Kubernetes. Third, Kubernetes also makes it really easy to secure traffic within your container networks and to or from the outside world. You can use Kubernetes to provide specific rules about how traffic to or from your containers is routed. You can even use Kubernetes to route traffic based on URLs, just like API gateways. Finally, Kubernetes can be used as a platform of platforms. The Kubernetes ecosystem is one of the things I love most. New tools and [[Microsoft Products|products]] to extend Kubernetes
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/level-up-even-more-with-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=185)** are created almost every day. These tools make Kubernetes even more powerful and flexible for all sorts of use cases. You can also use these tools to customize almost every detail of how your company's applications deploy and run.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Kubernetes]] (15), [[Routing]] (2), [[CLI]] (1), [[Security Risk]] (1), [[Jetpack Compose|Compose]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** docker (8), make (5), aws (1)
> **Env Vars:** cli (1), aws (1), api (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** kind of like (1), just like (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** vcenter (1)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Thank you](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/thank-you?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/thank-you?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Carlos] We made it to the end of the course. Great work. We did quite a lot. You should be proud of yourself. We went deep into how Docker and containers work. We learned about the relationship between containers, control groups, and namespaces, as well as the anatomy of container images. Speaking of container images, we learned how to create our own Dockerfile along with some advanced concepts to help us build faster and more secure container images. We saw how you could use container networks to connect your containers together and volumes to store data from your containers once their applications terminate. We also learned how to back-up and restore those volumes so that you never have to worry about losing important data. We combined these concepts to create Docker containers within Docker, and we learned about the difference between Docker in Docker and Docker through Docker. Finally, we learned more about what container image registries are by creating our own registry locally, both insecurely and securely. We also used all of this knowledge to create Docker containers within Docker and create images that work across multiple processors and operating systems. That is a lot of skills. Now that we know much more about how Docker works, be sure to give Docker [[Jetpack Compose|Compose]] a try if you're interested in running clusters of apps as containers on your machine. When you're ready, take your skills to the next level by learning how to run tons of containers with [[Kubernetes]]. Knowing how to use containers is an essential and highly marketable skill in today's ever-changing tech world. Be sure to keep practicing your newly acquired expertise, and remember to have fun.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/docker-essential-training/thank-you?u=76281980&t=94)** Good luck, and enjoy the journey.
